Diyah for False Zina Testimony: Liability of the Muzakki
Ruling on diyah when witnesses are disqualified and retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah liability when tazkiyah of zina witnesses is found invalid, identify the two conditions for the muzakki's liability, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The one who declares false witnesses trustworthy (muzakki) must pay diyah because tazkiyah is the cause of the cause; the Sahibayn disagree.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that if witnesses of zina are later found to be slaves or disbelievers, the one who performed tazkiyah – or the killer if the accused was killed by another method – is liable for diyah. The ruling is based on istihsan: the judge's order created a shubha preventing qisas. We will explore the reasoning and the internal Hanafi disagreement on the muzakki's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the facts and legal issue
Read the passage carefully. Note the two scenarios: (1) the accused is killed by a method other than stoning after the judge's order, and (2) someone performs tazkiyah on the witnesses, the accused is stoned, then the witnesses are found to be slaves or disbelievers. The legal issue: who is liable for diyah?
The passage also addresses partial retraction of witnesses.
2
Understand the Istihsan over Qiyas
The passage states that under qiyas, the killer would be subject to qisas (because he killed a protected life). However, istihsan applies because the judge's ruling created a shubha (semblance of legality), so qisas is replaced by diyah. This is the core proof: a shubha prevents the hadd or qisas.
This is a classic example of istihsan – preferring a weaker analogy due to a stronger consideration.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on muzakki liability
Abu Hanifa holds the muzakki liable because tazkiyah is like the cause of the cause (illat al-illah) – without it the testimony cannot proceed. The Sahibayn disagree: the muzakki only praised the witnesses; his speech does not directly establish zina. Note which view is mufta bihi.
The passage sides with Abu Hanifa, explaining that tazkiyah is essential for the testimony to become operative.
4
Apply the two conditions for muzakki's liability
The passage states two conditions: (1) the muzakki must have explicitly stated the witnesses' freedom and Islam (not just that they are 'adl), and (2) he must retract his tazkiyah by admitting deliberate falsehood (not error). If he persists or claims error, no liability.
These conditions are found in al-Fath and other commentaries; the matn itself is brief.

Source Text

كتاب الحدود باب شهادة الزنا والرجوع عنها
ربع ديته) ، فإنَّ المسألة فيما إذا كان الرجوع بعد الرجم"، والمعتبر بقاءُ مَن بقي ، لا رجوع من رجع ، وقد بقي ثلاثة أرباع النصاب.
وضَمِنَ الدِّيَةَ مَن قتل المأمور برجمه) : أي أُمِرَ بالرَّجم فقتله بطريق آخر، (أو زكي شهودَ الزّنا فرجم ، فظهروا عبيداً، أو كفّاراً فيهما) : أي في مسألة القتل والتزكية. بشهادته ثلاثة أرباع الحق، وهم ثلاثة شهود، وأصله أنّ المعتبر في باب الشهادة والرجوع بقاء من بقي لا رجوع من رجع على ما يأتي تفصيله إن شاء الله له في «كتاب الشهادة».

(1) قوله : ربع ديته ; فإن رَجَعَ الثالث ضمن الربع وحـد، ولو رجع الخمسة غرموا كل الدية أخماساً وحدّوا جميعاً. كذا في «النهر» [٢] قوله : بعد الرجم ; وأما الرجوع قبل الرجم فقد مر أنه يسقط عنه الرجم إذا لم يبق نصاب الشهادة وحدّ حد القذف كل من رجع. [٣] قوله : من قتل... الخ ; يعني إذا شَهِدَ أربعة على رجل بالزني فأمر القاضي برجمه، فضرب رجل آخر عنقه، ثمّ علم أن الشهود كانوا عبيداً أو كفاراً فعلى القاتل الدية، والقياس أن يجب القصاص ; لأنه قتل نفساً معصومة بغير حق. ووجه الاستحسان : إنّ القضاء بالرجم صحيح ظاهراً عند القتل، فأورث ذلك شبهة أسقطت القصاص، فإن قتله قبل القضاء وجب القصاص. كذا في «الهداية»(۳). [٤] قوله : بطريق آخر ; فإن قتله بذلك الطريق ; أي الرجم فلا شيء عليه. [0] قوله : أو زكّى ; ماض من التزكية، وهو معطوف على قتل، وهي عبارة عن وصف الشهود بأنهم أهل للشهادة. والحاصل : : أنه شهد أربعة بزنى على رجل، وزكّى المزكي الشهود فأمر القاضي برجمه فرجمَ ثمَّ ظهر أنهم عبيد أو كفّار، فعلى المزكي الدية.
وبيت المال إن لم يزك فرجم
والضمان على المزكين في قول أبي حنيفة . وعندهما : لا ضمان عليهم، بل في بيت المال.
وبيت المال إن لم يزك) فرجم) : أي ضَمِنَ بيت المال إذا شهدَ الشُّهودُ بالرجم، فلم يزكوا فرجم، فظهروا عبيداً، أو نحو ذلك
ويشترط لوجوب الضمان على المزكي أمران :
أحدهما : أن يكون أخبر بحرية الشهود وإسلامهم، فإن قال : علمت أنهم عدول ثم ظهر أنهم عبيد ، لم يضمن اتفاقاً ; لأنها ليست بتزكية، والاكتفاء بهذا القدر من خطأ القاضي. وثانيهما : أن يرجع المزكّي عن تزكيته قائلاً : تعمدت الكذب، فإن قال : أخطأت أو استمرّ على تزكيته فلا شيء عليه. كذا في «الفتح» (١) ، وبهذا يظهر ما في عبارة المتن من القصور، حيث اكتفى على ذكر الظهور.
[١]قوله : على المزكين ; اللام للجنس ; ليبطل بالجمعية، فإنّه لا يشترط في التزكية العدد ; ولذا أفرد المصنّف له صيغة زكي ; وذلك لأنّ الشهادة إنما تصير حجّة عاملة بالتزكية، فكانت التزكية في معنى علّة العلّة، فيضاف الحكم إليها عند تعذر إضافته إلى العلة، وبه يسقط ما قالاه من أنَّ المزكّي أثنى على الشهود خيراً وليس كلامه مثبتاً للزنى، وإنّما المثبت هو قول الشهود، فلا يجب عليه شيء عند رجوعه من ذلك الثناء. كذا في حواشي «الهداية»(۳).
[٢] قوله : إن لم يُزكّ; بصيغة المجهول ; أي إن لم تفعل التزكية وقبل القاضي شهادة الشهود فأمر بالرجم فرجمَ فظهر بعده أنّ الشهود كلهم أو بعضهم لم يكونوا ممن تقبل شهادته، فحينئذ ديته في بيت المال ; لأنّ الرجمَ كان بأمر القاضي، فينتقل فعلُ الراجمين إليه، والقاضي عامل للمسلمين، فالدية على مالهم لا على الراجمين ; لأنّ فعلهم منتقل إلى الآمر ولا على القاضي ; لأنّ الحاكم لا يضمن بنفسه فيما يفعل للمسلمين.
Book of Hudud, Chapter of Testimony of Zina and Retraction thereof. (He owes) a quarter of his diyah: the issue is when retraction occurs after stoning, and the consideration is the remaining (witnesses), not the one who retracted, and three-quarters of the (required) number remain. And the one who killed the person ordered to be stoned is liable for the diyah: meaning he was ordered to be stoned and he killed him by another method. Or (the one who) declared trustworthy (tazkiyah) the witnesses of zina and (the accused) was stoned, and then they appeared to be slaves or disbelievers in both cases: i.e. in the case of killing and tazkiyah... And the state treasury (is liable) if he did not perform tazkiyah and (the accused) was stoned. And liability is upon the ones who performed tazkiyah according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), there is no liability upon them, rather it is upon the state treasury.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses the liability for diyah (blood money) when the testimony of zina turns out to be false due to the witnesses being disqualified (slaves or disbelievers). The author first addresses the case where some witnesses retract after stoning: they pay the proportionate diyah. Then he addresses two scenarios: (1) a person kills the accused by another method after the judge ordered stoning, and (2) a person (muzakki) declares the witnesses trustworthy, they are stoned, and then the witnesses are found to be slaves or disbelievers. The ruling follows istihsan: the killer or muzakki must pay the diyah, because the judge's ruling created a semblance (shubha) that prevents qisas. Under pure qiyas, the killer would be subject to qisas, but istihsan reduces it to diyah. Similarly, the muzakki's tazkiyah is causally linked to the execution. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds the muzakki liable; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that the state treasury (Bayt al-Mal) is liable, not the muzakki, because the muzakki only praised the witnesses and did not directly establish the zina. The author then states two conditions for the muzakki's liability: (1) he must have explicitly stated the witnesses' freedom and Islam, and (2) he must retract his tazkiyah by saying he deliberately lied. If he claims error or persists, no liability. Finally, if no tazkiyah was performed at all, the diyah is on the state treasury because the execution was by the judge's order, and the judge's action is attributed to the Muslim community.

Key Terms
تزكية (tazkiyah): The act of declaring the witnesses trustworthy (adl) and qualified to testify, specifically stating their freedom and Islam.
ديته (diyatuhu): Blood money (diyah) owed by the one who caused the death of the accused erroneously, either by killing or by tazkiyah leading to stoning.
شبهة (shubha): A semblance of legality that negates the strict application of qisas and reduces it to diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for the muzakki's liability for diyah according to the passage?
A) The muzakki must have explicitly stated the witnesses' freedom and Islam.
B) The muzakki must retract his tazkiyah and admit he deliberately lied.
C) The muzakki must have performed tazkiyah before the stoning.
D) The muzakki must be a Muslim adult.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the muzakki is personally liable for diyah when his tazkiyah leads to erroneous stoning, provided he explicitly mentioned the witnesses' freedom and Islam and retracts admitting intentional falsehood.

Liability of Muzakki vs Bayt al-Mal in False Zina Testimony
Ruling on liability of certifiers and treasury in false zina testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the liability of the muzakki and Bayt al-Mal in false zina testimony, and apply the conditions for the muzakki's liability according to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Tazkiyah is the cause of the cause — liability attaches to the certifier when direct attribution is impossible.

What happens if witnesses in a zina case are later found to be disqualified? Who pays the blood-money? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the liability of the muzakki (the one who certified the witnesses) versus the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury). You will learn the conditions that trigger liability under Abu Hanifa's view and the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Read the opening sentence: 'والضمان على المزكين في قول أبي حنيفة . وعندهما : لا ضمان عليهم، بل في بيت المال.' Understand that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on who bears the daman (liability) when false testimony leads to a stoning.
The dispute is not about whether compensation is due, but who pays.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's conditions for liability on the muzakki
The passage lists two conditions: (1) The muzakki must have certified the freedom and Islam of the witnesses (not just their 'adalah). (2) He must retract his tazkiyah and state 'I intentionally lied.' If he says 'I made a mistake' or persists, he is not liable.
The text uses the verb 'تعمدت الكذب' showing deliberate falsehood is required.
3
Understand the rationale: tazkiyah as illat al-illah
The commentary explains that tazkiyah is like the cause of the cause (illat al-illah). The direct cause of stoning is the witnesses' testimony, but that testimony becomes operative only through tazkiyah. Hence, when the direct cause cannot be held liable (e.g., witnesses are dead or unknown), liability shifts to the certifier.
This is a Hanafi usul principle used to attribute rulings to secondary causes.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's position and Hanafi response
The Sahibayn argue that the muzakki merely praised the witnesses; his speech does not establish zina. The Hanafi response (footnote [1]) refutes this by using the illat al-illah concept, showing that liability can be attributed to the certifier.
Note that the lam in 'ala al-muzakkeen is for genus, meaning a single muzakki suffices.
5
Determine the ruling when no tazkiyah was performed
The second footnote (footnote [2]) explains that if no tazkiyah was done and the judge accepted the witnesses, then the diyah is on Bayt al-Mal because the judge's order transfers liability from the stoners to the judge as agent of the Muslims, and the judge does not personally guarantee.
This is the default case when the muzakki condition is absent.

وبيت المال إن لم يزك) فرجم) : أي ضَمِنَ بيت المال إذا شهدَ الشُّهودُ بالرجم، فلم يزكوا فرجم، فظهروا عبيداً، أو نحو ذلك
ويشترط لوجوب الضمان على المزكي أمران :
أحدهما : أن يكون أخبر بحرية الشهود وإسلامهم، فإن قال : علمت أنهم عدول ثم ظهر أنهم عبيد ، لم يضمن اتفاقاً ; لأنها ليست بتزكية، والاكتفاء بهذا القدر من خطأ القاضي. وثانيهما : أن يرجع المزكّي عن تزكيته قائلاً : تعمدت الكذب، فإن قال : أخطأت أو استمرّ على تزكيته فلا شيء عليه. كذا في «الفتح» (١) ، وبهذا يظهر ما في عبارة المتن من القصور، حيث اكتفى على ذكر الظهور.
[١]قوله : على المزكين ; اللام للجنس ; ليبطل بالجمعية، فإنّه لا يشترط في التزكية العدد ; ولذا أفرد المصنّف له صيغة زكي ; وذلك لأنّ الشهادة إنما تصير حجّة عاملة بالتزكية، فكانت التزكية في معنى علّة العلّة، فيضاف الحكم إليها عند تعذر إضافته إلى العلة، ، وبه يسقط ما قالاه من أنَّ المزكّي أثنى على الشهود خيراً وليس كلامه مثبتاً للزنى، وإنّما المثبت هو قول الشهود، فلا يجب عليه شيء عند رجوعه من ذلك الثناء. كذا في حواشي «الهداية»(۳).
[٢] قوله : إن لم يُزكّ; بصيغة المجهول ; أي إن لم تفعل التزكية وقبل القاضي شهادة الشهود فأمر بالرجم فرجمَ فظهر بعده أنّ الشهود كلهم أو بعضهم لم يكونوا ممن تقبل شهادته، فحينئذ ديته في بيت المال ; لأنّ الرجمَ كان بأمر القاضي، فينتقل فعلُ الراجمين إليه، والقاضي عامل للمسلمين، فالدية على مالهم لا على الراجمين ; لأنّ فعلهم منتقل إلى الآمر ولا على القاضي ; لأنّ الحاكم لا يضمن بنفسه فيما يفعل للمسلمين.
And Bayt al-Mal if he did not perform tazkiyah and they were stoned. Liability is on the muzakkis in the saying of Abu Hanifa. And according to the two: no liability upon them; rather upon Bayt al-Mal. And 'Bayt al-Mal if tazkiyah was not done and they were stoned' means: Bayt al-Mal is liable if the witnesses testified to stoning, and they did not perform tazkiyah and were stoned, and then they appeared to be slaves or the like. And two conditions are required for the obligation of liability upon the muzakki: One: That he had informed of the freedom and Islam of the witnesses. If he said: I knew they were upright, then they appeared to be slaves, he is not liable by agreement; because that is not tazkiyah, and relying on this extent of the judge's error. The second: That the muzakki retract his tazkiyah saying: I intentionally lied. If he said: I made a mistake, or he persisted in his tazkiyah, then nothing is upon him. Thus in al-Fath, and by this it is clear what in the text's expression is deficient, where it sufficed with mentioning the appearance. [1] His saying: 'upon the muzakkis'; the lam is for the genus; to invalidate the plurality; indeed number is not required in tazkiyah; therefore the author used the singular form 'zakkā'. That is because testimony only becomes operative proof through tazkiyah, so tazkiyah is like the cause of the cause, so the ruling is attributed to it when attribution to the cause is impossible. And by this, what the two said falls: that the muzakki praised the witnesses and his speech is not establishing zina, rather the establisher is the speech of the witnesses, so nothing is obligatory upon him when he retracts that praise. Thus in the margins of al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: 'if tazkiyah was not done' in the passive voice; meaning if tazkiyah was not performed and the judge accepted the witnesses' testimony and ordered stoning and they were stoned, then afterwards it appeared that all or some of the witnesses were not among those whose testimony is accepted, then his blood-money is in Bayt al-Mal; because the stoning was by the judge's order, so the action of the stoners is transferred to him, and the judge is an agent for the Muslims, so the blood-money is upon their wealth, not upon the stoners; because their action is transferred to the orderer, nor upon the judge; because the ruler does not personally guarantee what he does for the Muslims.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the liability (daman) when witnesses in a zina case are later found to be disqualified (e.g., slaves). The core ruling: According to Abu Hanifa, the one who certified the witnesses (muzakki) is liable to pay the blood-money (diyah) if he later retracts his certification and admits he lied. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), there is no liability on the muzakki; instead, the blood-money is paid from the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury). The Sharh then explains two conditions for the muzakki's liability under Abu Hanifa's view: (1) The muzakki must have explicitly stated the witnesses' freedom and Islam (not just their 'adalah/uprightness); (2) He must retract his certification by saying 'I intentionally lied.' If he says 'I made a mistake' or does not retract, he is not liable. The commentary in the footnotes provides further rationale: Tazkiyah is like the 'cause of the cause' (illat al-illah) — the testimony is the direct cause of the stoning, but the certification enables the testimony, so liability attaches to the certifier. The Sahibayn's counter-argument that the certifier is merely praising and not establishing zina is refuted by this causation chain. The second footnote explains that if certification was not done at all, the blood-money is on Bayt al-Mal because the judge's order transferred liability from the stoners to the judge as agent of the Muslims, and the judge does not personally guarantee.

Key Terms
تزكية (tazkiyah): Certification of a witness's uprightness ('adalah) by a qualified person (muzakki).
مزكّ (muzakki): The person who performs tazkiyah (certifies witnesses).
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay compensation (here: blood-money/diyah) for harm caused by false testimony.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Muslim state.
علّة العلّة (illat al-illah): The cause of the cause; a secondary effective cause that is not direct but is linked to the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, which of the following is a condition for the muzakki's liability?
A) The muzakki must certify the witnesses' 'adalah only.
B) The muzakki must retract his certification and say 'I intentionally lied'.
C) The muzakki must be the only person to certify the witnesses.
D) The witnesses must be found to be slaves.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the liability for diyah in false zina testimony lies either on the muzakki (if he intentionally lied) or on Bayt al-Mal (if no certification or the Sahibayn's view is followed), based on the concept of illat al-illah.

Testimony for Ihsan: Intentional Looking and the Ihsan Dispute
Ruling and dispute on ihsan proof and intentional looking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on accepting testimony of one man and two women for ihsan and evaluate the opposing arguments of al-Shafi'i and Zafar.

Core Idea
Ihsan is a set of virtuous qualities, not a cause; thus women's testimony is accepted by general rules.

This lesson explores two intertwined issues from the chapter on hudud: the effect of intentional looking by witnesses on their testimony, and the proof of ihsan (marital status) required for stoning. The Hanafi school permits intentional looking for testimony unless done for lust. More importantly, we examine a key inter-madhhab dispute: can ihsan be proven by the testimony of one man and two women? The Hanafi position is that it can, while Imam al-Shafi'i and Zafar disagree. We will walk through the reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sub-issues
The passage first addresses if witnesses who intentionally looked at the private parts for testimony are still credible. Then it shifts to proving ihsan when the accused denies it. Separate these two rulings.
The first ruling is straightforward; the second involves a major dispute.
2
Understand the intentional-looking ruling
If witnesses confess they looked intentionally to bear testimony, their testimony is accepted unless they admit lustful intent (taladhdhudh), which makes them fasiq. Note the analogies to medical necessity.
3
State the Hanafi position on ihsan proof
The Hanafi school holds that ihsan can be proven by one man and two women. Their testimony leads to stoning. This is the mufta bihi.
Contrast with the opposing views.
4
Analyse the opposing proofs
Al-Shafi'i rejects women's testimony in non-financial matters. Zafar argues ihsan is like a cause (illa) because it aggravates the penalty, so only male witnesses suffice, analogizing to the case of two dhimmis testifying to a prior emancipation.
Follow the logic of each side.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response and tarjih
The Hanafi response: ihsan is not a cause but a set of qualities that prevent zina. The general rule allowing women's testimony applies. The analogy to dhimmis is rejected because in that case, the date (pre-zina) is contested, not the emancipation itself.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi would stone; others would not.

Source Text

كتاب الحدود باب شهادة الزنا والرجوع عنها
وإن شهدوا بزنا وأقروا بنظرهم عمداً ،قبلت ، وزان أنكر وطء عرسه
[١] قوله : قبلت ; إلا إذا قالوا : تعمدنا النظر إلى العورة للتلذذ فحينئذ لا تقبلُ (1) لفسقهم. كذا في «الفتح». [٢] قوله : لتحمل الشهادة ; يعني نظر الشهود لهذا الغرض إلى عورتي الزانيين ليتحملوا الشهادة ويؤدّوها إلى الحاكم مباح ، فلا يعرضُ لهم الفسخ به.
ومثله نظر القابلة والخافضة والختان والطبيب إذا كان المرض بموضع لا يحل النظر إليه، وكذا يجوز النظر إلى العورة عند الاحتقان، ورؤية البكارة في العنّة، والردّ بالعيب.

منه
ولدت منه ; فإنه ير معناه أن ينكر الدخول بعد وجودِ سائر الشرائط ; لأنّ الحكم بثباتِ النَّسب . حكم بالدخول عليه ; ولهذا لو طلقها يُعْقِبُ الرجعة والإحصانَ يَثْبُتُ بمثله، فإن لم تكن ولدت منه وشهد عليه بالإحصانِ رجل وامرأتان رجم خلافاً لزفر والشافعي فالشافعي ه مر على أصله إنّ شهادتهنَّ غير مقبولة في غير الأموال، وزفر له يقول : إنّه شرط في معنى العلة ; لأن الجناية تتغلّظ عنده ، فيضافُ الحكم إليه، فأشبه حقيقة العلة، فلا تقبل شهادة النساء فيه ، فصار كما إذا شهد ذميان على ذمي زنى عبده المسلم أنه أعتقه قبل الزنا، فلا تقبل لما ذكرنا.
ولنا : إنّ الإحصان عبارة عن الخصال الحميدة، وإنّها مانعة من الزنا، فلا يكون في معنى العلّة، وصار كما إذا شهدوا به في غير هذه العلّة، بخلاف ما ذكر ; لأنّ العتق يثبت بشهادتهما ، وإنما لا يثبت بسبق التاريخ ; لأنه ينكره المسلم أو يتضرر به المسلم
Book of Hudud, Chapter of Testimony of Zina and Retraction Therefrom. And if they testified to zina and confessed that they looked intentionally, it is accepted; and if the adulterer denies intercourse with his wife... [1] His saying: 'it is accepted' except if they said: we intentionally looked at the private part for lust, then it is not accepted due to their fisq. Thus in al-Fath. [2] His saying: 'to bear testimony' means the witnesses' looking for this purpose at the private parts of the two adulterers to bear testimony and perform it to the judge is permissible, so they are not rendered fasiq by it. Similar is the looking of the midwife, the circumciser, the circumciser, and the doctor if the illness is in a place where looking is not permissible, and likewise looking at the private part is permissible for enema, and seeing the hymen in impotence, and return due to defect. Thus in al-Fath. [3] His saying: 'and if the adulterer denies intercourse with his wife' is the subject, its predicate is 'he is stoned'. The sentence 'he denies intercourse with his wife' ... said in al-Hidayah: 'If four testify against a man for zina and he denies ihsan, and he has a wife who has borne him a child, then he is stoned.' Its meaning is that he denies penetration after existence of all other conditions; because the ruling of establishing lineage is a ruling of penetration upon him; thus if he divorces her, it entails the right of return, and ihsan is established by similar. If she has not borne a child from him and they testify to his ihsan by one man and two women, he is stoned, contrary to Zafar and al-Shafi'i. Al-Shafi'i adheres to his principle that women's testimony is not accepted in other than financial matters. Zafar says: it is a condition in the meaning of cause; because the crime is aggravated according to him, so the ruling is attributed to it, so it resembles the actual cause, so women's testimony is not accepted in it, becoming like when two dhimmis testify against a dhimmi that his Muslim slave freed him before the zina, it is not accepted for what we mentioned. And for us: Ihsan is an expression of praiseworthy qualities, and they prevent zina, so it is not in the meaning of cause, and it becomes like when they testify in other than this cause, unlike what was mentioned; because emancipation is established by their testimony, but it is not established by prior date because the Muslim denies it or is harmed by it.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage covers two sub-topics within the chapter on zina testimony. First: The ruling on witnesses who confess that they intentionally looked at the private parts of the accused in order to bear testimony. The Sharh clarifies that their testimony is accepted unless they admit they looked for sexual gratification (taladhdhudh), which would make them fasiq and disqualify them. Looking for testimony is permissible, and analogies are drawn with doctors, midwives, and other cases where necessity permits looking at the 'awrah. Second: The requirement of ihsan (marital status) for the penalty of stoning. The matn provides: if a man denies having intercourse with his wife (i.e., denies being muhsan), but he has a child from her, he is considered muhsan because the child establishes intercourse. If there is no child, and ihsan is proved by the testimony of one man and two women, the Hanafi school accepts this testimony and the accused is stoned. This is disputed by Imam al-Shafi'i, who does not accept women's testimony in hudud, and by Zafar, who considers ihsan to be like a condition in the meaning of a cause for the aggravated penalty, thus requiring male witnesses by analogy to the case where two dhimmis testify to a prior emancipation. The Hanafi response is that ihsan is not a cause but a set of virtuous qualities that prevent zina; it is merely a prerequisite, so women's testimony is admissible by general rules.

Key Terms
إحصان (ihsan): Marital status of being free, adult, Muslim, and having consummated a valid marriage.
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony given in court to establish a fact.
عورة ('awrah): Parts of the body that must be covered and are normally forbidden to look at.
حد (hadd): Fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Shariah for certain crimes, such as zina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school accept testimony of two women and one man for proving ihsan?
A) Because ihsan is a financial matter.
B) Because ihsan is a set of praiseworthy qualities, not a cause, so general rules of testimony apply.
C) Because the accused consented.
D) Because the Shafi'i school also accepts it.

Key Takeaway
Ihsan is a qualifying set of virtues, not a legal cause, thus women's testimony suffices in Hanafi fiqh.

Acceptance of Testimony When Witnesses Admit Intentional Looking
Ruling on testimony acceptance with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the acceptance of testimony when witnesses admit to intentional looking for the purpose of bearing testimony, and identify the exception that invalidates the testimony.

Core Idea
Intentional looking for testimony is permissible; but if done for pleasure, testimony is rejected due to fisq.

In zina cases, the law requires four witnesses who actually saw the act. This lesson clarifies a procedural issue: what if the witnesses admit that they intentionally looked at the private parts of the accused? The Hanafi school holds that such testimony is accepted, provided the looking was for the legitimate purpose of bearing testimony. However, if they confess it was for sexual pleasure, their testimony is rejected because they become sinners (fasiq). This ruling highlights the importance of intention (niyya) in determining the validity of testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states that if witnesses testify to zina and admit they looked intentionally, their testimony is accepted.
The phrase 'qabila' means accepted.
2
Note the exception
The exception is when they say they looked for pleasure; then their testimony is not accepted because they are fasiq.
Fisq disqualifies a witness.
3
Understand the rationale
Looking for testimony is permissible (mubah) because it is a necessary means to fulfill a legal obligation. This is analogized to other permissible lookings: midwife, doctor, etc.
The principle: means to an obligation are permissible.
4
Distinguish from forbidden looking
If the looking is accompanied by a forbidden intention (pleasure), it becomes haram and the testimony is rejected.
Intention changes the ruling of the act.

[2] His statement: 'condition in the sense of the effective cause' summary: Ihsan, although it is a condition for stoning, is in the sense of the effective cause, like zina, so just as women's testimony is not accepted in establishing the effective cause, it is not accepted in it either. Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the Sharh al-Wiqaya deals with two distinct rulings within the chapter on testimony for zina. The first ruling: If four witnesses testify to an act of zina and admit that they intentionally looked at the private parts of the accused, their testimony is accepted. However, if they confess that they looked for sexual pleasure, they are considered sinful and their testimony is rejected. This is based on the principle that looking for the purpose of bearing testimony is permissible, similar to the permissibility for midwives or doctors to look at private parts for medical necessity. The second, more extensive ruling concerns the legal concept of ihsan (being muhsan) and the admissibility of women's testimony to prove it. The Hanafi school holds that if two men and two women testify to a man's ihsan, and the man denies it, the testimony is accepted and he is stoned. This positions the Hanafis against al-Shafi'i and Zufar. Al-Shafi'i rejects all women's testimony in non-financial matters, while Zufar argues that ihsan is a condition that functions like an effective cause (illa) for stoning, thus women's testimony is inadmissible. The Hanafi response distinguishes ihsan from the effective cause: ihsan is a set of virtuous qualities that prevent zina, not a cause that leads to stoning. It is merely a condition, and conditions can be proven by women's testimony. The text further clarifies that if the wife gives birth within six months after the act, that establishes consummation and thus ihsan. The Hanafi position is that women's testimony is acceptable for ihsan because it is not part of the core crime (zina) but a secondary condition.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahada): Testimony; legal evidence given in court. In zina, requires four witnesses.
فسق (fisq): Sinfulness or moral corruption; a fasiq person is not acceptable as a witness in Hanafi fiqh.
مباح (mubah): Permissible; an act that is neither commanded nor prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is testimony rejected even if witnesses saw the act?
A) If they admit they looked intentionally
B) If they admit they looked for pleasure
C) If they are not four in number
D) If they are related to the accused

Key Takeaway
Testimony is accepted if witnesses looked intentionally for testimony; it is rejected if they admit looking for pleasure.

Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Women's Testimony for Ihsan
Inter-madhhab dispute on women's testimony for ihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on accepting women's testimony for ihsan in zina cases, and evaluate the opposing arguments from al-Shafi'i and Zufar.

Core Idea
Ihsan is a condition, not a cause; Hanafis accept women's testimony for conditions, while Shafi'is reject it in non-financial matters.

In zina cases, the punishment of stoning requires the offender to be muhsan (married and having consummated). Establishing ihsan is crucial. The Hanafi school holds that ihsan can be proven by the testimony of two men and two women. This contradicts al-Shafi'i and Zufar, who reject women's testimony in hudud-related matters. This lesson unpacks the debate, focusing on whether ihsan is a 'condition' or an 'effective cause' of stoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Hanafis: If two men and two women testify to a man's ihsan, and he denies it, he is stoned. Women's testimony is accepted for proving ihsan.
Ihsan consists of marriage and consummation.
2
Identify the opposing schools
Al-Shafi'i rejects women's testimony in non-financial matters entirely. Zufar (Hanafi dissenter) argues that ihsan is a condition in the sense of the effective cause (illa), so women's testimony is inadmissible.
Zufar's view is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i's principle: women's testimony is only accepted in financial matters (amwal). Zufar: since ihsan makes the crime more severe (stoning instead of lashes), it is like an effective cause, and women cannot testify to causes.
Zufar analogizes ihsan to the act of zina itself.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis: Ihsan is not an effective cause; it is a set of praiseworthy qualities that prevent zina. It does not lead to the stoning but is a condition. Conditions can be proven by women's testimony.
They compare to other conditions like 'previous acquittal' which can be proven by women.
5
Determine the practical consequence
In Hanafi school, a defendant can be stoned based on women's testimony for ihsan. In Shafi'i and Zufar's view, such testimony is rejected, so unless male witnesses alone prove ihsan, the punishment is lashes.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the evidentiary threshold for stoning.

And if she has given birth from him, or if a man and two women testify to his ihsan, he is stoned, and she has given birth from him, or if a man and two women testify to his ihsan, he is stoned. This is according to us, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. Zufar made ihsan a condition in the sense of the effective cause, so women's testimony is not accepted in it. [1] His statement: 'she gave birth from him' means his wife before zina, and likewise if she gave birth after zina within less than six months, then his lineage is established and he is considered the fornicator. [2] His statement: 'condition in the sense of the effective cause' summary: Ihsan, although it is a condition for stoning, is in the sense of the effective cause, like zina, so just as women's testimony is not accepted in establishing the effective cause, it is not accepted in it either. And we say: Ihsan is not in the sense of the effective cause, for the effective cause is that which leads to the effect, and ihsan is not like that.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the Sharh al-Wiqaya deals with two distinct rulings within the chapter on testimony for zina. The first ruling: If four witnesses testify to an act of zina and admit that they intentionally looked at the private parts of the accused, their testimony is accepted. However, if they confess that they looked for sexual pleasure, they are considered sinful and their testimony is rejected. This is based on the principle that looking for the purpose of bearing testimony is permissible, similar to the permissibility for midwives or doctors to look at private parts for medical necessity. The second, more extensive ruling concerns the legal concept of ihsan (being muhsan) and the admissibility of women's testimony to prove it. The Hanafi school holds that if two men and two women testify to a man's ihsan, and the man denies it, the testimony is accepted and he is stoned. This positions the Hanafis against al-Shafi'i and Zufar. Al-Shafi'i rejects all women's testimony in non-financial matters, while Zufar argues that ihsan is a condition that functions like an effective cause (illa) for stoning, thus women's testimony is inadmissible. The Hanafi response distinguishes ihsan from the effective cause: ihsan is a set of virtuous qualities that prevent zina, not a cause that leads to stoning. It is merely a condition, and conditions can be proven by women's testimony. The text further clarifies that if the wife gives birth within six months after the act, that establishes consummation and thus ihsan. The Hanafi position is that women's testimony is acceptable for ihsan because it is not part of the core crime (zina) but a secondary condition.

Key Terms
إحصان (ihsan): The legal status of being muhsan, requiring free adulthood, marriage, and consummation; necessary for stoning as punishment for zina.
علة (illa): Effective cause; the reason that brings about a ruling. In zina, the act itself is the illa for hadd punishment.
شرط (shart): Condition; a prerequisite for a ruling to apply, but not the cause that generates the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school accept women's testimony for proving ihsan in zina cases, according to the passage?
A) Because ihsan is part of the effective cause of stoning
B) Because ihsan is a condition, not an effective cause, and conditions can be proven by women's testimony
C) Because the Shafi'i school also accepts women's testimony for ihsan
D) Because the Quran explicitly mentions women's testimony for ihsan

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts women's testimony for ihsan because it is a condition, not the effective cause of stoning.

Women's Testimony for Ihsan in Zina
Dispute over acceptance of women's testimony to establish ihsan for stoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on accepting women's testimony for ihsan, distinguish between a condition (shart) and a cause (illa), and evaluate the opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Ihsan is a condition, not a cause — women's testimony is thus accepted for it.

In this lesson, we examine a critical dispute in Hanafi fiqh: can women testify to establish the condition of ihsan (being married) that makes an adulterer liable for stoning? The Hanafi school answers yes, while Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i say no. The disagreement hinges on whether ihsan is a cause (illa) for the punishment or merely a condition (shart). Understanding this distinction is essential for mastering the logic of hadd penalties.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafis hold that testimony of one man and two women suffices to prove ihsan, leading to stoning.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that ihsan is in the meaning of the cause (fi ma'na al-'illa) for stoning, so women's testimony is not accepted, just as it is not accepted for the zina itself.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
They analogize ihsan to zina itself: since women's testimony is not accepted for establishing the cause (zina), it should not be accepted for ihsan, which they consider part of the cause.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that ihsan is not a cause but a condition. A cause leads to the effect, while the condition merely describes the perpetrator. Stoning is caused by zina, not by ihsan. Therefore, the analogy fails.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, if a man and two women testify to someone's ihsan, Hanafis accept it and stone the person; Zufar and Shafi'i would not accept it unless four male witnesses testify to ihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد وَلَدَتْ منه، أو شَهِدَ بإحصانِه رجل وامرأتان رجم) (۱) : هذا عندنا خلافاً لزفر والشَّافِعِي (۲) الله، وزُفَر الله جعل الإحصان شرطاً في معنى العلة (۳) فلا تقبل فيه شهادة النساء
[ ١ ] قوله : ولدت منه؛ أي زوجته قبل الزنى، وكذا لو ولدت بعد الزني لدون ستة أشهر، فإنّه يثبت نسبه فيجعلُ واطئاً.
(۲) قوله : شرطاً في معنى العلة؛ حاصله : إنّ الإحصان وإن كان شرطاً للرجم لكنه في معنى العلة، كالزنى، فكما لا تقبل شهادة النِّساءِ في إثباتِ العلة لا تقبل فيه أيضاً.
ونحن نقول : إنّ الإحصانَ ليس في معنى العلّة، فإنّ العلة ما تكون مفضية إلى المعلول، والإحصان ليس كذلك.
And if she gave birth from him, or if a man and two women testified to his ihsan, he is stoned. This is our view, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. Zufar made ihsan a condition in the meaning of the cause, so women's testimony is not accepted therein. [1] His saying: 'gave birth from him' means his wife before the zina, and likewise if she gave birth after zina within less than six months, then his lineage is established and he is considered a fornicator. [2] His saying: 'a condition in the meaning of the cause' — its summary: that ihsan, although a condition for stoning, is in the meaning of the cause, like zina. So just as women's testimony is not accepted in establishing the cause, it is not accepted therein either. And we say: ihsan is not in the meaning of the cause, because the cause is that which leads to the effect, and ihsan is not like that.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the acceptance of women's testimony to establish ihsan (the condition of being married and having consummated marriage) for the hadd of stoning. The Hanafi position, as stated here, is that if a man and two women testify to the defendant's ihsan, the defendant is stoned, even though the testimony includes women. This is contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who argue that ihsan is like a cause (`illa) for stoning, and since women's testimony is not accepted for establishing the cause itself (such as the act of zina), it should not be accepted for ihsan either. The Hanafi response distinguishes ihsan from a true cause: a cause (illa) is that which directly leads to the effect (the hadd), whereas ihsan is merely a condition (shart) that makes the person eligible for stoning after zina has already occurred. It does not lead to the stoning; rather, it qualifies the act. Therefore, women's testimony can be accepted for ihsan as it is not in the meaning of the cause.

Key Terms
إحصان (ihsan): In the context of zina, it refers to the condition of being a free, adult Muslim who has had lawful sexual intercourse within a valid marriage.
علة (ʿilla): The effective cause that leads to a legal effect; here, it is argued whether ihsan is like a cause for stoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis accept women's testimony for establishing ihsan, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i do not?
A) Because Hanafis consider ihsan a cause for stoning.
B) Because Hanafis consider ihsan a condition, not a cause, so the prohibition of women's testimony for causes does not apply.
C) Because women's testimony is accepted in all hudud matters according to Hanafis.
D) Because the Quran explicitly allows women's testimony for ihsan.

Key Takeaway
The admissibility of women's testimony for ihsan hinges on whether ihsan is classified as a condition (shart) or part of the cause (illa); Hanafis treat it as a condition, thereby allowing such testimony.

Hadd for Drinking: 80 Lashes and Its Proof
Opening the chapter on the punishment for drinking, stating its measure and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the punishment of drinking liquid intoxicants, cite the primary proofs (hadith and ijmāʿ), and distinguish between hadd and taʿzīr for solid intoxicants.

Core Idea
The punishment for drinking alcohol is 80 lashes, established by hadith and the consensus of the Companions.

In this passage, the commentator opens the chapter on the hadd for drinking by immediately equating it to the hadd for qadhf (false accusation of adultery) – both are 80 lashes. But the proof structure differs: qadhf is from the Qur'an, while the measure for drinking is derived from the practice of the Companions, especially 'Umar, and corroborated by the Prophet's command to flog the drinker. This lesson unpacks how the Hanafi school arrived at this ruling and why it applies only to liquids.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Start by noting the punishment: 80 lashes for a free person. Understand that the term 'shurb' is defined as consuming a liquid intoxicant; solid intoxicants fall outside this hadd and receive ta'zir.
The comparison with qadhf (also 80 lashes) is a mnemonic and establishes equivalence in number.
2
Examine the primary proof
Look at the marfu' hadith: 'If he drinks, flog him...' This command establishes the obligation to flog but does not specify the number. The number comes from the practice of the Companions.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for establishing the general obligation.
3
Understand the consensus (ijma)
The passage cites the precedent of Abu Bakr (40 lashes) and 'Umar (80 lashes after consultation). The consensus of the Companions, especially 'Umar's decision with the endorsement of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, fixed the number at 80.
This ijma is a strong proof, making the ruling mufta bihi.
4
Distinguish between hadd and ta'zir
Note the linguistic point: shurb is specific to liquids. Therefore, solid intoxicants (e.g., opium) are not subject to hadd but to ta'zir (discretionary punishment).
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school and has practical implications.
5
Note the ikhtilaf on the fourth offense
The hadith mentions death on the fourth drink. The passage presents two views: abrogated or left as siyasa (ruler's discretion). The more correct view is that it remains as siyasa.
This affects how judges handle repeat offenders: the fixed hadd does not increase, but the ruler may impose a harsher discretionary penalty at the fourth time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حد الشرب
هو كحد القذف ثمانون سوطاً للحرّ
[١] قوله : باب حدّ الشرب؛ - بضم الشين -؛ أي شرب المسكر، وهو يختص بالمائعات، فإنّ تناول المسكرات الغير السيّالة كالأفيون ونحوه، يقال له : الأكل لا الشرب؛ ولهذا قالوا : لا يُحَدُّ بالسكر منه، بل يعزر.
[٢] قوله : هو كحد القذف؛ شبهه به؛ لأنّ حد القذف ثبت بالقرآن، وحد الشرب أصله ثبت بالأحاديث المرفوعة، وتقديره ثبت باتفاق الصحابة، فأخرج ابنُ ماجة وأبو داود والنسائي مرفوعاً: «إذا سكر فاجلدوه، ثمّ إن سكر فاجلدوه، ثم إن سكر فاجلدوه، فإن عاد الرابعة فاقتلوه» (۱)، ومثله أخرجه ابن حبان في «صحيحه»، وعبد الرزاق في «مصنّفه»، والحاكم في مستدركه» وقال : صحيح على شرط الشيخين. وفي الباب أخبار كثيرة مبسوطةٌ في تخريج أحاديث الهداية»(۲) للزيلعي، والأمرُ بالقتل في المرة الرابعة قيل : إنه منسوخ وقيل : إنّه باق سياسة، وهو الأصح. وفي «صحيح البخاري عن السائب له : «كنا نؤتى بالشارب على عهد رسول الله وأمـرة أبي بكر وصدر من خلافة عمر له فنقوم إليه بأيدينا ونعالنا وأرديتنا حتى كان آخر إمرة عمر الله له فجلد أربعين حتى إذا عتوا وفسقوا جلد ثمانين» (۳). وفي «صحيح مسلم عن أنس ل له : «إنّ النبي الله جلد في الخمر بالجريد والنعال، ثم جلد أبو بكر له أربعين، فلما كان عمر له ودنى الناس من الريف والقرى، قال : ما ترون في جلد الخمر، فقال عبد الرحمن بن عوف الله : أرى أن تجعله كأخف الحدودِ فجلد عمر ثمانين سوطا (٤).
Chapter of the Hadd for Drinking
It is like the hadd of qadhf: eighty lashes for a free person.
[1] His saying: 'Chapter of the hadd for drinking' — with a dammah on the shin — meaning: drinking an intoxicant, and it is specific to liquids. For consuming non-liquid intoxicants like opium and the like, it is called eating, not drinking; hence they said: one is not subject to hadd due to intoxication from it, but is punished by ta'zir.
[2] His saying: 'It is like the hadd of qadhf' — he compares it to it because the hadd of qadhf is established by the Quran, and the hadd for drinking is originally established by marfu' hadiths, and its measure is established by the consensus of the Companions. Ibn Majah, Abu Dawud, and al-Nasa'i reported as marfu': 'When he drinks, flog him; then if he drinks, flog him; then if he drinks, flog him; and if he returns a fourth time, kill him.' Similarly, it was narrated by Ibn Hibban in his Sahih, 'Abd al-Razzaq in his Musannaf, and al-Hakim in his Mustadrak, who said: 'Sahih according to the condition of al-Bukhari and Muslim.' There are many other hadiths on the topic detailed in al-Zayla'i's Takhrij Ahadith al-Hidayah. The command to kill on the fourth offense is said to be abrogated, and it is said that it remains as a discretionary punishment (siyasa), and this is the more correct view. In Sahih al-Bukhari from al-Sa'ib: 'We used to bring the drinker during the time of the Messenger of Allah, the caliphate of Abu Bakr, and the early part of 'Umar's caliphate, and we would strike him with our hands, sandals, and garments, until at the end of 'Umar's caliphate he flogged forty lashes. Then when people became rebellious and wicked, he changed to eighty lashes.' In Sahih Muslim from Anas: 'The Prophet flogged for wine with palm branches and sandals. Then Abu Bakr flogged forty. When 'Umar came and people drew near to cultivated lands and villages, he said: What do you think about flogging for wine? 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf said: I think you should make it like the lightest of the hudud. So 'Umar flogged eighty lashes.'
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the hadd punishment for drinking intoxicants. It first states the ruling: the punishment is eighty lashes for a free person, likening it to the hadd of qadhf (false accusation of adultery). The commentary explains that the term 'shurb' (drinking) specifically applies to liquids; solid intoxicants like opium are not subject to this hadd but are punished with ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The proof for the eighty lashes is derived from a number of hadiths: the famous hadith about flogging the drinker up to the fourth time (with the death penalty for the fourth, which is considered either abrogated or left for the ruler's discretion), and the practice of the Companions. Initially, the punishment was administered informally (with hands, sandals, garments), then Abu Bakr established 40 lashes, and eventually 'Umar, after consulting the Companions, fixed it at 80 lashes as the lightest of the hudud (since the hadd for qadhf is also 80 lashes). This became the consensus. The passage highlights that the measure is based on the consensus of the Companions (ijma' al-sahaba), which is a strong proof.

Key Terms
حد الشرب (hadd al-shurb): The fixed corporal punishment for consuming liquid intoxicants; eighty lashes for a free person.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment not fixed by shari'ah, applied for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or when the hadd conditions are not met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the basis for setting the hadd for drinking at 80 lashes?
A) The Quranic verse on qadhf.
B) The Prophet's direct command specifying 80 lashes.
C) The consensus of the Companions, particularly 'Umar's practice.
D) Qiyas on the hadd for theft.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the punishment for drinking liquid intoxicants is 80 lashes for a free person, based on the Prophet's command to flog and the consensus of the Companions.

Hadd for Drinking Alcohol: 80 Lashes for Free, 40 for Slave
Ruling and proof for hadd of drinking alcohol

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hadd punishment for drinking alcohol in the Hanafi school, identify the proofs (ijma and qiyas) that establish it, and list the conditions required for its application.

Core Idea
The Hanafi hadd for drinking is 80 lashes for a free person, established by consensus and analogy with the hadd of slander.

This passage opens the chapter on the fixed punishment for drinking alcohol. It declares that the punishment is 80 lashes for a free person, similar to the hadd for false accusation (qadhf). The commentator then provides the evidence: the practice of the early Companions and the reasoning of Ali ibn Abi Talib. This lesson will dissect the ruling, its proofs, and the conditions for its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the opening line: the hadd is 80 lashes for the free person. Note that for a slave it is half, 40 lashes.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the Primary Proof (Ijma)
The passage cites the hadith of al-Sa'ib in Sahih al-Bukhari, showing the practice of the Companions from the Prophet's time until Umar's later caliphate, where the number increased from 40 to 80. This indicates a consensus (ijma) on 80 lashes.
The text also mentions a hadith about flogging up to four times, but the focus here is on the agreed number.
3
Understand the Secondary Proof (Qiyas)
The passage includes Ali's reasoning: drinking leads to intoxication, which leads to raving, which leads to slander (qadhf). Since qadhf carries 80 lashes, drinking should carry the same. This is an analogy (qiyas) with the asl of qadhf.
The illa (effective cause) is the progression to slander.
4
List the Conditions for Application
The hadd applies only if: the drinker is a Muslim, legally responsible (mukallaf), not coerced, not in necessity; the substance is khamr (fermented grape juice) or another intoxicant that causes intoxication; and the act is voluntary.
For non-khamr, the hadd requires actual intoxication, not just consumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حد الشرب
هو كحد القذف ثمانون سوطاً للحرّ
[١] قوله : باب حدّ الشرب ; أي شرب المسكر، وهو يختص بالمائعات، فإنّ تناول المسكرات الغير السيّالة كالأفيون ونحوه ، يقال له : الأكل لا الشرب ؛ ولهذا قالوا : لا يُحَدُّ بالسكر منه، بل يعزر.
[٢] قوله : هو كحد القذف ; شبهه به ; لأنّ حد القذف ثبت بالقرآن، وحد الشرب أصله ثبت بالأحاديث المرفوعة، وتقديره ثبت باتفاق الصحابة ، فأخرج ابنُ ماجة وأبو داود والنسائي مرفوعاً: «إذا سكر فاجلدوه، ثمّ إن سكر فاجلدوه، ثم إن سكر فاجلدوه، فإن عاد الرابعة فاقتلوه» (۱) ، ومثله أخرجه ابن حبان في «صحيحه» ، وعبد الرزاق في «مصنّفه»، والحاكم في مستدركه» وقال : صحيح على شرط الشيخين. وفي الباب أخبار كثيرة مبسوطةٌ في تخريج أحاديث الهداية»(۲) للزيلعي، والأمرُ بالقتل في المرة الرابعة قيل : إنه منسوخ وقيل : إنّه باق سياسة، وهو الأصح. وفي «صحيح البخاري عن السائب له : «كنا نؤتى بالشارب على عهد رسول الله وأمـرة أبي بكر وصدر من خلافة عمر له فنقوم إليه بأيدينا ونعالنا وأرديتنا حتى كان آخر إمرة عمر الله له فجلد أربعين حتى إذا عتوا وفسقوا جلد ثمانين» (۳). وفي «صحيح مسلم عن أنس ل له : «إنّ النبي الله جلد في الخمر بالجريد والنعال ، ثم جلد أبو بكر له أربعين ، فلما كان عمر له ودنى الناس من الريف والقرى ، قال : ما ترون في جلد الخمر، فقال عبد الرحمن بن عوف الله : أرى أن تجعله كأخف الحدودِ فجلد . ثمانين سوطا (٤).
ونصفها للعبد بشرب الخمر ولو قطرة فمَن أُخِذَ بريحها وإن زالت لبعد الطريق
[٤] وفي موطأ مالك» : «إنّ عمره استشار في الخمر ، فقال له علي بن أبي طالب : نرى أن تجلدَه ثمانين، فإنّه إذا شرب سكر ، وإذا سكر هذى، وإذا هذى افترى ، وعلى المفتري ثمانون، فاجعله حدّ الفرية، فجلد عمر الله ثمانين)، ومثله أخرجه الحاكم وصححه، وعبد الرزاق والدارقطني وغيرهم، وتفصيل هذا البحث ليطلب من «التعليق الممجّد على موطأ محمد (۲).
[١] قوله : ونصفها ; أي أربعون سوطاً، كما في حد القذف، قال الزهري: «إنّ عليّاً وعمر وعثمان وابن عامر الله جلدوا عبيدهم في الخمـر نـصـف حـد الحـر في الخمر» (۳) ، أخرجه مالك في «الموطأ»، وأخرج أيضاً عن عبد الله بن عامر بن ربيعة الله قال: «أدركت عثمان بن عفان والخلفاء هلم جرا ، فما رأيت أحداً ضرب عبداً في فرية أكثر من أربعين»(4).
[٢] قوله : بشرب ; إذا كان الشرب من غير اضطرار كحالة مخمصة، وغير إكراه، وكان الشاربُ مسلماً مكلّفاً ناطقاً غير أخرس. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره.
[٣] قوله : الخمر ; هو النِّيءُ من ماء العنب إذا غلا واشتدّ وقذف بالزبد وما سواه من الأشربة لا يُحَدُّ بشرب قطرة منه ، بل إذا سكر منه، وسيأتي تفصيله في «كتاب الأشربة».
[٤] قوله : وإن زالت الضمير إلى الريح، وتأنيثه لكونه مؤنثاً سماعياً ، يعني : وإن زالت الرائحة لبعدِ الطريق إلى الحاكم ، قال في «الهداية»: «فإن أخذه الشهود وريحها يوجد منه أو هو سكران فذهبوا به من مصر إلى مصر فيه الإمام، فانقطع ذلك قبل أن ينتهوا به حُدَّ في قولهم جميعاً ; لأنّ هذا عذر كبعد المسافة في حد الزنا، والشاهد لا يأثم به في مثله»
Chapter of the hadd of drinking. It is like the hadd of qadhf: eighty lashes for the free person. [1] He said: Chapter of hadd of drinking—meaning drinking intoxicants, and it is specific to liquids, for consuming non-liquid intoxicants like opium etc. is called eating not drinking; hence they said: no hadd is incurred by intoxication from it, rather he is given a discretionary punishment. [2] He said: It is like the hadd of qadhf—he compared it to it because the hadd of qadhf is established by the Quran, and the hadd of drinking's origin is established by raised traditions (ahadith marfu‘ah), and its amount is established by the agreement of the Companions. Ibn Majah, Abu Dawud, and al-Nasa'i narrated marfu‘: 'If he becomes intoxicated, flog him; then if he becomes intoxicated, flog him; then if he becomes intoxicated, flog him; then if he returns a fourth time, kill him.' Similarly, it was narrated by Ibn Hibban in his Sahih, Abd al-Razzaq in his Musannaf, and al-Hakim in his Mustadrak and said: authentic according to the condition of the two shaykhs. There are many traditions in this chapter detailed in Takhrij Ahadith al-Hidayah by al-Zayla‘i. The command to kill on the fourth occasion: it is said it is abrogated, and it is said it remains as a policy, and this is the most correct. In Sahih al-Bukhari from al-Sa'ib: 'We used to bring the drinker to the Messenger of Allah and the caliphate of Abu Bakr and the early part of 'Umar's caliphate, and we would stand up to him with our hands, sandals, and cloaks, until the end of 'Umar's caliphate, he flogged forty, then when they became rebellious and immoral, he flogged eighty.' In Sahih Muslim from Anas: 'The Prophet flogged for wine with branches and sandals, then Abu Bakr flogged forty, then when 'Umar came and people approached the countryside and villages, he said: What do you think about flogging for wine? Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf said: I think you should make it like the lightest of the hudud, so he flogged eighty lashes.' And half of it for the slave for drinking wine, even a drop. Then whoever is caught by its smell even if it fades due to distance of the road. [4] In Muwatta Malik: 'Umar consulted about wine, and 'Ali ibn Abi Talib said: We think you should flog him eighty, because if he drinks he becomes intoxicated, if he becomes intoxicated he raves, if he raves he slanders, and the slanderer has eighty, so make it the hadd of slander. So 'Umar flogged eighty.' Similar was narrated by al-Hakim and authenticated, and by Abd al-Razzaq and al-Daraqutni. Details of this research may be sought from al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad. [1] He said: And half of it—meaning forty lashes, as in the hadd of qadhf. Al-Zuhri said: 'Ali, 'Umar, 'Uthman, and Ibn 'Amir flogged their slaves for wine half the hadd of the free for wine.' Narrated by Malik in Muwatta. Also narrated from 'Abdullah ibn 'Amir ibn Rabi'a: 'I witnessed 'Uthman and the caliphs successively, and I never saw anyone strike a slave for slander more than forty.' [2] He said: For drinking—if the drinking is without necessity like hunger, and without coercion, and the drinker is a Muslim, legally responsible, speaking, not mute. So in al-Bahr and others. [3] He said: Wine—it is the raw juice of grapes when it boils, becomes strong, and foams. As for other drinks, no hadd is incurred by drinking a drop of it, but only if one becomes intoxicated from it. Its detail will come in the Book of Drinks. [4] He said: And if it fades—the pronoun refers to the smell, and its femininity because it is a feminine noun by usage, meaning even if the smell fades due to the distance of the road to the judge. He said in al-Hidayah: 'If the witnesses seize him and his smell is found on him or he is intoxicated, and they take him from one city to another where the imam is, and that disappears before they reach him, he is flogged according to all of them, because this is an excuse like the distance of travel in the hadd of zina, and the witness does not sin thereby.'
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah presents the ruling on the fixed punishment (hadd) for drinking alcohol. It opens the chapter (bab) on the hadd of drinking, establishing that the punishment is 80 lashes for a free person, analogous to the hadd for false accusation (qadhf). The commentator explains that the hadd of qadhf has a clear Quranic basis, while the hadd for drinking is rooted in the Prophetic traditions (marfu‘ hadith) and its specific number was set by the consensus of the Companions (ijma‘ al-sahabah). Supporting narrations are cited: a hadith from the Prophet commanding flogging for intoxication up to a fourth offense (with a debate on its abrogation), and reports from the practice of the early caliphs showing a progression from 40 to 80 lashes. The passage then states that a slave receives half the punishment (40 lashes), supported by the practice of several Companions recorded in the Muwatta’ of Imam Malik. Conditions for the application of the hadd are specified: the drinker must be a Muslim, mature, sane, and capable of speech, and the act must be voluntary without necessity (like hunger) or coercion. The definition of 'khamr' is given as the fermented juice of grapes; other intoxicating drinks only incur the hadd if intoxication is achieved. Finally, the commentary addresses a procedural issue: if witnesses detect the smell of alcohol on the accused but the smell fades during transit to the judge, the hadd is still applied, as this is comparable to the distance excuse in the hadd of zina. This ruling is drawn from Al-Hidayah and reflects the standard Hanafi position.

Key Terms
حد الشرب (hadd al-shurb): The fixed punishment for drinking alcohol, set at 80 lashes for a free person and 40 for a slave.
خمر (khamr): The raw juice of grapes that ferments, becomes strong, and foams. It is the only intoxicant for which drinking a single drop incurs the hadd.
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The fixed punishment for falsely accusing a chaste person of zina, set at 80 lashes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the hadd punishment for a free person who drinks alcohol?
A) 40 lashes
B) 80 lashes
C) 100 lashes
D) 20 lashes

Key Takeaway
The hadd for a free person is 80 lashes, based on the consensus of the Companions and analogy with qadhf; for a slave it is 40, and conditions such as being Muslim, voluntary, and the definition of khamr must be met.

Hadd for a Slave Drinking Khamr
Ruling on slave's hadd for drinking khamr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hadd for a slave who drinks khamr and explain the proof from the Companions' practice.

Core Idea
A slave receives forty lashes for drinking khamr — half the free person's hadd, based on the practice of the Companions.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah establishes the specific punishment for a slave who drinks khamr. It provides the evidence from the actions of the Caliphs Umar and Ali, who set eighty lashes for free persons, from which the slave's punishment is derived by analogy to qadhf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on the slave's hadd
The text explicitly says 'wa nisfaha lil'abd' meaning half of the free person's 80, so 40 lashes. Note that this applies even for a single drop.
2
Locate the proof (dalil) from the Companions
The narration from Umar consulting Ali and then flogging eighty establishes the free person's hadd. The hadd for the slave is derived by logical extension (qiyas) as the companions flogged slaves forty in practice.
The proof is from the practice of the Companions (qawl sahabi).
3
Understand the legal analogy
Ali's reasoning: drinking leads to intoxication, which leads to slander, so the hadd of slander (80 lashes) is applied. The slave's hadd is half by analogy to the principle of applying half-hadd in certain cases.
This is a qiyas jaliy.
4
Confirm the ruling from multiple reports
Al-Zuhri reports that Ali, Umar, Uthman, and Ibn Amir flogged their slaves forty lashes. This consensus among prominent Companions strengthens the ruling.
5
Note the conditions for the hadd to be applied
The drinking must be voluntary, without coercion or necessity, and the drinker must be Muslim, competent, and speaking. These conditions are from the later part of the passage.
These are conditions (shurut) for the hadd, not for the slave's penalty specifically.

ونصفها للعبد بشرب الخمر ولو قطرة. فمن أخذ بريحها وإن زالت لبعد الطريق. وفي موطأ مالك: إن عمر استشار في الخمر، فقال له علي بن أبي طالب: نرى أن تجلده ثمانين، فإنه إذا شرب سكر، وإذا سكر هذى، وإذا هذى افترى، وعلى المفتري ثمانون، فاجعله حد الفرية، فجلد عمر ثمانين. ومثله أخرجه الحاكم وصححه، وعبد الرزاق والدارقطني وغيرهم. وتفصيل هذا البحث ليطلب من التعليق الممجّد على موطأ محمد. [1] قوله: ونصفها؛ أي أربعون سوطاً، كما في حد القذف. قال الزهري: إن علياً وعمر وعثمان وابن عامر جلدوا عبيدهم في الخمر نصف حد الحر في الخمر. أخرجه مالك في الموطأ، وأخرج أيضاً عن عبد الله بن عامر بن ربيعة قال: أدركت عثمان بن عفان والخلفاء هلم جرا، فما رأيت أحداً ضرب عبداً في فرية أكثر من أربعين. [2] قوله: بشرب؛ إذا كان الشرب من غير اضطرار كحالة مخمصة، وغير إكراه، وكان الشارب مسلماً مكلّفاً ناطقاً غير أخرس. كذا في البحر وغيره. Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains the hadd (fixed punishment) for a slave who drinks khamr (wine). The main ruling is that the slave receives half the punishment of a free person, i.e., forty lashes, based on the practice of the Companions as recorded in the Muwatta. The passage then details that the proof for the offense can be established by the smell of khamr on the person, even if that smell dissipates while being transported to the judge. This is analogous to the case of zina where distant travel is an excuse. The conditions for the hadd to apply are that the drinking must be voluntary, without coercion or necessity (like starvation), and the drinker must be a legally responsible Muslim who can speak. Khamr is specifically defined as raw grape juice that ferments; other intoxicating drinks do not incur the hadd from a single drop but only upon intoxication. This ruling is supported by the narrations from the Muwatta showing the actions of the early caliphs and the opinion of Ali.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): The fixed corporal punishment prescribed by the Sharia for specific offenses; here, the punishment for drinking khamr is eighty lashes for a free person and forty for a slave.
الريح (al-rih): The smell of khamr that serves as evidence of consumption; if present on the accused, the hadd can be applied even if the smell later dissipates.
الخمر (al-khamr): Intoxicating drink made from raw grape juice that has fermented and frothed. Other drinks are not called khamr and require intoxication for the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 40 lashes
C) 20 lashes
D) No hadd if only a drop

Key Takeaway
The slave's hadd for drinking khamr is forty lashes, half of the free person's, established by the consensus of the Companions.

Proof of Drinking Khamr by Lingering Smell
Ruling on proof by lingering smell of khamr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the remaining smell of khamr is sufficient evidence for the hadd, even after dissipation, and identify the conditions for its application.

Core Idea
The persistent smell of khamr on the accused suffices for the hadd, even if it fades during transport — analogous to distance in zina.

This passage addresses a practical evidentiary rule: the hadd for drinking can be based on the smell of khamr on a person, even if that smell disappears while being taken to the judge. The Hidayah compares this to the case of zina where a similar excuse applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule about smell
The text states 'man ukhidha bi rihiha' — whoever is caught by its smell, meaning the smell is sufficient evidence.
2
Understand the allowance for dissipation
The phrase 'wa in zalat libu'd al-tariq' indicates that even if the smell dissipates due to distance, the hadd is still applied.
This prevents the accused from escaping punishment simply because the evidence fades during transport.
3
Analyze the analogy from al-Hidayah
Al-Hidayah draws an analogy to the hadd for zina, where if witnesses travel with the accused and lose sight of the act, the hadd is still applied. This shows that practical difficulties in maintaining evidence do not remove the hadd.
This is a qiyas: asl = zina, far = khamr, illa = difficulty of maintaining evidence over distance.
4
Recognize the conditions for this proof
The witnesses must have initially detected the smell or seen intoxication. The accused must have been seized while the smell was present.
5
Contrast with other madhahib
This Hanafi rule is distinctive; other schools may require the smell to persist at the time of judgment or additional evidence.

Hadd for Date Wine and Requirement of Confession or Testimony
Rules for nabiidh and necessity of confession/witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for proving the hadd for date wine, including the requirement of confession or testimony and the intra-Hanafi dispute on the number of confessions.

Core Idea
Hadd for non-grape intoxicants requires proof of intoxication plus confession or testimony; a single confession suffices according to the majority.

This passage explains the evidential requirements for imposing the hadd (fixed punishment) for drinking date wine (nabidh) and similar intoxicants. It clarifies that mere observation of intoxication or smell is insufficient; either a valid confession or witness testimony is needed. The commentary also highlights an internal Hanafi disagreement over how many confessions are required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act triggers the hadd (drinking date wine) and the overall ruling (hadd becomes wajib upon proper proof).
The text specifies that for nabiidh, actual intoxication must be proven, not just consumption.
2
Distinguish khamr from other intoxicants
For khamr (grape wine), the presence of smell is sufficient proof; for other intoxicants like nabiidh, intoxication must be established.
This distinction is critical for understanding the threshold of proof.
3
Identify the modes of proof
The hadd is not imposed merely by catching someone intoxicated. There must be either a confession (iqrar) or the testimony of two male witnesses (shahada).
Copy variants have 'or' for confession, but the correct reading is 'and' meaning both are needed? Actually 'or' is correct: either confession or testimony suffices.
4
Analyze the number of confessions required
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that one confession suffices; Abu Yusuf requires two. The text favors the majority view.
The phrase 'هذا عندهما' indicates the position of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that a single confession is enough.
5
Understand the validity of confession while intoxicated
A confession made while intoxicated is invalid. However, if the accused was taken to a distant city and became sober, his later confession is accepted.
This addresses a practical scenario to illustrate the rule's nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو سكران زائل العقل بنبيذ التمر، وأقر به مرة، أو شَهِدَ به [1] قوله: أو سكران؛ أي أخذ الشارب وهو سكران زال عقله بشرب غير الخمر، ففيه إشارة إلى أن المعتبر في الخمر هو وجود الريح، وإن لم يوجد السكر، وفي غيره: السكر، فلا يحد بشرب المقدار الغير المسكر. [2] قوله: نبيذ التمر؛ أي بشرب نبيذ التمر، وهو أن يلقي تمر في ماء إلى أن تخرج فيه حلاوته ويشتد، وذكره اتفاقي، وإلا فالحكم كذلك في نبيذ الزبيب والعسل، ونحو ذلك من المائعات المسكرة. نعم لو سكر بما يباح شربه لا يحد. ذكره الزيلعي. [3] قوله: وأقر؛ في بعض النسخ أو أقر، وهو ليس بصحيح، فإن الأخذ بوجود الرائحة في الخمر وبحالة السكر في غيره لا يكفي في وجوب الحد، حتى يقر به الشارب أو تشهد عليه الشهود. [4] قوله: مرة؛ هذا عندهما، وعند أبي يوسف لا يشترط الإقرار مرتين. [5] قوله: أي بشرب الخمر؛ أو بالسكر بالنبيذ، هو بالضم، مصدر معطوف على الشرب، وهو أيضاً مصدر. واعترض عليه: بأن الإقرار بالسكر بالنبيذ لا يجوز حال السكر لعدم اعتبار إقرار السكران على ما سيأتي، ولا بعد زوال السكر للتقادم كما صرحوا به، فالأولى أن يرجع ضمير به إلى شرب الخمر فقط. وأجيب عنه: بأن الإقرار بالسكر يعتبر بعد زواله في بعض الصور، كما إذا أخذ سكران فذهبوا به إلى مصر فيه الإمام، فزال سكره لبعد الطريق. [6] قوله: أو شهد؛ عطف على قوله أقر، والحاصل أن الأخذ حال سكره أو وجود الرائحة لا يوجب الحد، ما لم ينضم معه إقراره أو شهادة الشهود.
Or an intoxicated person whose mind is gone due to date wine, and he confessed once, or it was testified against him... [1] His saying: 'or intoxicated'; i.e., the drinker is caught while intoxicated, his mind gone from drinking other than khamr. This indicates that for khamr, the criterion is the presence of the smell, even if intoxication is not found, and for other drinks, the criterion is intoxication, so he is not punished for drinking a non-intoxicating amount. [2] His saying: 'date wine'; i.e., by drinking date wine, which is to put dates in water until its sweetness emerges and it becomes strong. Mentioning it is accidental, otherwise the ruling is the same for raisin wine, honey wine, and similar intoxicating liquids. Indeed, if someone becomes intoxicated from what is permissible to drink, he is not punished. Al-Zayla'i mentioned it. [3] His saying: 'and he confessed'; in some copies 'or he confessed', but this is not correct. Because catching by the presence of smell for khamr and by the state of intoxication for other drinks is not sufficient to impose the hadd until the drinker confesses or witnesses testify against him. [4] His saying: 'once'; this is according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), whereas Abu Yusuf does not require confession twice. [5] His saying: 'i.e., by drinking khamr; or by intoxication from date wine' – the word 'sukr' is a masdar coordinated with 'shurb'... It is objected that confession of intoxication from date wine is not valid while intoxicated because the confession of an intoxicated person is not considered, nor after sobriety due to the passage of time, so it is better that the pronoun 'bi' refers only to drinking khamr. And it is answered that the confession of intoxication is considered after sobriety in some cases, such as when he is taken while intoxicated and they take him to the imam in another city, and his drunkenness wears off due to the distance. [6] His saying: 'or testified against him' is coordinated with 'he confessed'. The conclusion is that apprehending him while intoxicated or the presence of smell does not necessitate the hadd unless accompanied by his confession or witnesses' testimony.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the hadd for drinking, now focusing on intoxicants other than khamr, specifically date wine (nabidh). The ruling distinguishes that for khamr, the mere presence of the smell is sufficient proof, but for other intoxicants, actual intoxication must be established. The hadd is not applied for drinking a small amount that does not intoxicate. The text clarifies that the hadd cannot be imposed solely based on catching the person while intoxicated or finding the smell; there must be either a confession from the drinker or testimony from witnesses. Regarding confession, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the 'two'), a single confession suffices, while Abu Yusuf requires two confessions. The commentary also addresses the issue of a confession made while intoxicated: it is generally invalid, but if the person was taken to a distant city and became sober on the way, his later confession may be accepted. This shows the school's detailed approach to procedural evidence in hudud.

Key Terms
نبيذ التمر (nabidh al-tamr): Date wine: an intoxicating drink made by soaking dates in water until fermentation occurs. It is mentioned as an example; raisin wine and honey wine have the same ruling.
سكران (sakran): An intoxicated person: one who has lost his reason due to consuming an intoxicant. For the hadd to apply, his confession must be made when sober, not while intoxicated.
إقرار (iqrar): A confession made by the accused. In the context of hudud, it is a means of proving the offense. For hadd al-shurb, a single confession suffices according to the majority.

B) Twice
C) Three times
D) No confession needed if caught intoxicated

Key Takeaway
For hadd al-shurb with non-khamr intoxicants, actual intoxication must be proven through a valid confession (one suffices per the majority) or testimony of witnesses; mere observation of intoxication is insufficient.
