The Evidentiary Distinction Between Khamr and Other Intoxicants for Hadd
Defines sukran and distinguishes khamr from other intoxicants for hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for establishing hadd for drinking khamr versus other intoxicants, and apply it to new scenarios.

Core Idea
For khamr, smell suffices; for other intoxicants, intoxication must be proven.

The hadd punishment for drinking is a fixed penalty, but its application depends on the type of intoxicant. This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies a crucial distinction: pure wine (khamr) is treated differently from other intoxicating beverages like date wine. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the hadd in legal practice according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of intoxicants
The passage distinguishes between khamr (grape wine) and other intoxicants (e.g., nabidh al-tamr). Recognize that the ruling differs for each.
Khamr is specifically grape wine; other intoxicants include date wine, raisin wine, honey wine, etc.
2
Understand the evidentiary rule for khamr
For khamr, the existence of its characteristic smell (reeh) is sufficient to prove drinking and apply hadd, even if the person is not intoxicated.
The passage says: 'المعتبر في الخمر هو وجود الريح'.
3
Understand the evidentiary rule for other intoxicants
For non-khamr intoxicants, actual intoxication (sukr) must be proven; drinking a non-intoxicating amount does not incur hadd.
The passage says: 'وفي غيره: السكر'.
4
Note the exception
If a person becomes intoxicated by something that is inherently permissible to drink (e.g., a small amount of a drink that becomes intoxicating in large quantities), no hadd is applied.
Al-Zayla'i's exception: 'لو سكر بما يباح شربه لا يحد'.
5
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Practice distinguishing between khamr and other intoxicants in hypothetical cases to determine whether the hadd applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو سكران زائل العقل بنبيذ التمر، وأقر به مرة، أو شَهِدَ به
قوله : أو سكران ؛ أي أُخِذَ الشارب وهو سكران زال عقله بشرب غير الخمر، ففيه إشارة إلى أنّ المعتبر في الخمر هو وجود الريح ، وإن لم يوجد السكر، وفي غيره : السكر، فلا يحدّ بشرب المقدار الغير المسكر.
قوله : نبيذ التمر ؛ أي بشرب نبيذ التمر، وهو أن يلقي تمر في ماء إلى أن تخرج فيه حلاوته ويشتد، وذكره اتفاقي، وإلا فالحكم كذلك في نبيذ الزبيب والعسل، ونحو ذلك من المائعات المسكرة، نعم لو سكر بما يباح شربه لا يحد، ذكره الزَّيْلَعِي.
Or an intoxicated person whose intellect is removed by date wine, and he confessed to it once, or witnesses testified to it. He said: 'Or an intoxicated person' means the drinker was taken while intoxicated, his intellect removed by drinking other than wine. In this is an indication that for wine, the consideration is the existence of its smell, even if intoxication is not found; and for other [intoxicants], intoxication [is considered]. So he is not punished by the hadd for drinking a non-intoxicating amount. He said: 'Date wine' means drinking date wine, which is that dates are thrown into water until their sweetness comes out and it becomes strong. Its mention is incidental; otherwise the ruling is the same for raisin wine, honey wine, and similar intoxicating liquids. Yes, if one becomes intoxicated by something whose drinking is permissible, he is not punished by hadd. Al-Zayla'i mentioned it.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling distinction. The matn states the case of an intoxicated person who drank date wine (nabidh al-tamr) and then confessed or was witnessed. The commentary (sharh) explains that the term 'sukran' (intoxicated) here refers to someone whose intellect is removed by a drink other than pure wine (khamr). The key ruling is that for khamr (grape wine), the mere presence of its distinctive smell ('reeh') is sufficient to establish that the person drank it, regardless of whether he is intoxicated. For other intoxicants like nabidh, the hadd punishment is only incurred if the person actually becomes intoxicated; drinking a non-intoxicating amount does not trigger the hadd. The commentary also clarifies that nabidh al-tamr (date wine) is just one example; the same rule applies to raisin wine, honey wine, and similar intoxicating liquids. An important exception: if a person becomes intoxicated by something that is inherently permissible to drink (e.g., small amounts of a liquid that is not khamr but becomes intoxicating in large quantities), he is not punished by hadd. This distinction is specific to the Hanafi school's approach to proving drinking for hadd.

Key Terms
سكران (sukran): An intoxicated person, whose intellect is removed by an intoxicating drink other than khamr (wine).
نبيذ التمر (nabidh al-tamr): Date wine: an intoxicating beverage made by soaking dates in water until the water becomes sweet and strong.
خمر (khamr): Wine, specifically the intoxicating drink from grapes, but used generically for any intoxicant. In this passage, it refers to grape wine whose smell is indicative.
ريح (reeh): The smell or odor of wine (khamr), which is considered a proof of drinking in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The person must be intoxicated.
B) The presence of the smell of wine must be detected.
C) Two witnesses must testify to actual drinking.
D) The person must confess twice.

Key Takeaway
For wine, the distinctive smell alone triggers the hadd; for other intoxicants, actual intoxication is required.

Confession and Testimony Requirements for Hadd al-Shurb: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains need for confession or testimony and intra-Hanafi disagreement on number of confessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why physical evidence alone is insufficient for hadd al-shurb and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the number of required confessions.

Core Idea
Hadd is not established by smell or intoxication alone; confession or testimony is essential, and Hanafis differ on whether one or two confessions suffice.

The hadd for drinking intoxicants is a severe punishment, and Hanafi jurists are cautious to apply it only when the evidence is unequivocal. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that mere physical signs—like the smell of wine or visible intoxication—are not sufficient. Confession by the drinker or testimony of two male witnesses is required. Within the Hanafi school, there is an internal disagreement over whether one confession is enough or two are needed. The text also addresses a subtle issue: can a confession made while intoxicated be accepted after sobriety, or does delay (taqadum) bar it? Let's unpack these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The text states that taking someone with the smell of wine or in a state of intoxication does not obligate the hadd until confession or testimony is added. So physical signs alone are never sufficient.
2
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on confessions
The matn says 'once' (marra). According to the 'two' (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), one confession suffices. However, Abu Yusuf also holds a separate opinion requiring two confessions. This creates a practical consequence: with one confession, the hadd is applied; with two required, it may be averted if only one is given.
The text does not resolve which position is mufta bihi, but the standard Hanafi view is one confession.
3
Analyze the linguistic objection and its resolution
The phrase 'by drinking wine or by intoxication from nabidh' seems problematic because confession to intoxication while drunk is invalid, and after sobriety it may be rejected due to delay. The resolution is that in some cases, like when the intoxicated person is transported to the imam and his intoxication wears off en route, the confession can be accepted after sobriety.
This shows the school's flexibility to avoid injustice.
4
Understand the testimony requirement
Witnesses must be sober and testify to both the act of drinking and the presence of the smell at the time of testimony. The judge may order an examination of the person's breath.
Testimony of women is not accepted in hadd cases due to the principle of daf' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وأقر؛ في بعض النسخ أو أقرّ، وهو ليس بصحيح، فإنّ الأخذ بوجودِ الرائحة في الخمر وبحالة السكر في غيره لا يكفي في وجوب الحد، حتى يقر به الشارب أو تشهد عليه الشهود.
قوله : مرة؛ هذا عندهما، وعند أبي يوسف له يشترط الإقرار مرتين.
قوله : أي بشرب الخمر؛ أو بالسكر بالنبيذ، هو – بالضم -، مصدر معطوف على الشرب، وهو أيضاً مصدر، واعترض عليه : بأن الإقرار بالسكر بالنبيذ لا يجوز حـال السكر لعدم اعتبار إقرار السكران على ما سيأتي، ولا بعد زوال السكر للتقادم كما صرحوا به، فالأولى أن يرجع ضمير به إلى شرب الخمر فقط. وأجيب عنه : بأن الإقرار بالسكر يعتبر بعد زواله في بعض الصور، كما إذا أُخِذ سكران فذهبوا به إلى مصر فيه الإمام، فزال سكره لبعد الطريق.
قوله : أو شهد؛ عطفٌ على قوله : «أقرّ »، والحاصل أنَّ الأخذ حال سكره أو وجود الرائحة لا يوجب الحد، ما لم ينضم معه إقراره أو شهادة الشهود، والشهادة
(أي صاحياً؛ لأن إقرار السكران بالشرب لا يعتبر لقوة احتمال الكذب في كلامه، فلا يعتبر فيما يندرى بالشبهة. ينظر : فتح باب العناية (٣: ٢٢٥).
بكل منهما أي شرب الخمر والسكر من غيره مقيّدة بوجود الرائحة، فلا بد مع شهادتهما بالشرب أن يثبت عند الحاكم أنّ الريح قائم حال الشهادة، وهو بأن يشهدا به وبالشرب أو به فقط، فيأمر القاضي باسْتِنْكَاهِهِ. كذا في «الفتح»
He said: 'And he confessed' – in some copies 'or he confessed', which is not correct. Indeed, taking [the person] with the existence of the smell in wine and with the state of intoxication in other [drinks] is not sufficient for the obligation of hadd until the drinker confesses to it or witnesses testify against him. He said: 'once' – this is according to the two, and according to Abu Yusuf, two confessions are required. He said: 'i.e., by drinking wine' or 'by intoxication from nabidh' – it is with a dammah, a verbal noun coordinated with 'drinking', which is also a verbal noun. And an objection was raised: that confession to intoxication from nabidh is not allowed while intoxicated because the confession of an intoxicated person is not considered, as will come, nor after the intoxication wears off due to the passage of time (taqadum) as they have clarified. So it is better that the pronoun 'bi-hi' refer only to drinking wine. And it was answered: that confession to intoxication is considered after it wears off in some cases, such as when an intoxicated person is taken and they take him to a city where the imam is, and his intoxication wears off due to the distance of the road. He said: 'or witnesses testified' – coordinated with his saying 'he confessed'. The upshot is that taking [him] while he is intoxicated or with the existence of the smell does not necessitate the hadd unless his confession or the testimony of witnesses is joined with it. And testimony (i.e., while sober; because the confession of an intoxicated person about drinking is not considered due to the strong probability of falsehood in his speech, so it is not considered in matters that are averted by doubts. See: Fath Bab al-Inayah 3:225). By each of them, i.e., drinking wine and intoxication from other [drinks] is conditioned by the existence of the smell. So it is necessary, along with their testimony about drinking, that it be established before the judge that the smell was present at the time of testimony. That is by them testifying about [the smell] and about drinking, or about [the smell] alone; then the judge orders him to be smelled. Thus in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and elaboration on the requirements for establishing the hadd for drinking. The matn states the need for confession or testimony. The commentary first corrects a variant reading, emphasizing that taking a person with the smell of wine or in a state of intoxication is not enough by itself; confession or testimony is necessary. It then addresses a dispute regarding the number of confessions: the phrase 'once' (marra) is interpreted according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) as one confession being sufficient, but according to Abu Yusuf (alone) two confessions are required. This is a known internal Hanafi ikhtilaf. Next, the commentary discusses a linguistic objection: the phrase 'by drinking wine or by intoxication from nabidh' is problematic because confession to intoxication from nabidh is not valid while drunk, nor after sobriety due to delay (taqadum). The answer is that it can be valid after sobriety in some scenarios, e.g., if the person was taken intoxicated and transported to the imam far away, so his intoxication wears off en route. The commentary also states the general principle: physical signs (smell or intoxication) alone do not establish the hadd; confession or testimony is required. Finally, it notes that for testimony, the smell must be present at the time of testimony; the judge may order an examination of the person's breath. This reflects the Hanafi school's strict evidentiary requirements, avoiding capital or corporal punishment on mere appearances.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession by the accused that he drank the intoxicant, which is one of the ways to establish the hadd.
شهادة (shahada): Testimony of two male witnesses who saw the person drink or detected the smell, establishing the hadd.
تقادم (taqadum): Delay; the passing of time between the act and the confession or testimony, which may bar acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One confession
B) Two confessions
C) Four confessions
D) No confession needed

Key Takeaway
Hadd al-shurb requires either confession (one or two) or testimony of two witnesses; physical signs are not sufficient.

Conditions and Procedural Nuances of Hadd al-Shurb
Ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on conditions and procedural details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions required for applying hadd al-shurb and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of delay (taqadum) and the ruling on vomiting.

Core Idea
Hadd al-shurb requires two male witnesses, voluntary drinking, and sobriety; delay and vomiting create procedural doubts that may avert the punishment.

The hadd punishment for drinking intoxicants is a fixed penalty that must be applied with strict procedural safeguards to avoid error. This passage from the Hanafi tradition outlines the essential conditions for its application—testimony, voluntariness, and sobriety—and explores two nuanced scenarios: when time has passed (taqadum) and when the drinker vomits. Understanding these conditions illuminates the Hanafi commitment to the principle of averting punishments through doubts (yudra' bi al-shubuhat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three explicit conditions
The matn states that the hadd is applied when: (1) there are two male witnesses, (2) the drinking was voluntary (taw'an), and (3) the person is sober (sahiyan). Note the exclusion of women's testimony and the requirement of volition.
These conditions are from the zahir al-riwayah.
2
Understand the rationale for each condition
Women's testimony is not accepted in hadd cases because hadd is averted by doubts, and women's testimony is considered weaker. Compulsion (ikrah) drops the hadd, as in zina. Sobriety is required because the punishment aims to deter, which is impossible when intoxicated.
The commentator links these to the broader principle of dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on delay (taqadum)
When the smell of alcohol disappears before confession or testimony, the passage presents a dispute. For testimony, delay prevents acceptance by agreement, but its measure differs: Muhammad says one month; the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say until the smell disappears. For confession, the two say delay invalidates it, while Muhammad says it does not.
This dispute shows the tension between relying on physical evidence (smell) and a fixed time limit.
4
Examine the ruling on vomiting
If a person vomits wine but does not confess and no one testifies, he is not punished. The commentator explains this is because the vomiting alone does not prove the drinking was voluntary or that the necessary conditions are met.
This highlights that outward signs without additional evidence are insufficient for hadd.
5
Synthesize the overall principle
The common thread is that hadd is a severe punishment and must be applied only with certainty. Any doubt—whether from missing conditions, delay, or incomplete evidence—averts the punishment.
This aligns with the famous maxim: 'al-hudud tudra' bi al-shubuhat' (hudud are averted by doubts).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجلان، وعُلِمَ شربُهُ طوعاً يحد صاحياً.
قوله : رجلان؛ أشار به إلى عدم اعتبار شهادة المرأة، إذ لا اعتبار لشهادتهن فيما يندرئ بالشبهات، ولا بد للإمام أن يسأل الشهود عن الماهية والكيفية والمكان والزمان على ما مر في حد الزنى.
قوله : طَوعاً؛ - بفتح الطاء المهملة -؛ أي بلا إكراه، فلو شربه مكرهاً سقط عنه الحد، كما في حد الزنى.
قوله : صاحياً؛ اسم فاعل من الصحو، هو ضدّ السكر بالفارسية هوشياري : أي يُحد في حال الصحو لا في حال كونه سكران؛ لأن الحد شُرِعَ زاجراً، ولا يُتَصوَّرُ ذلك حالة السكر.
قوله : فإن أقر ...الخ؛ إنما لا يُحدّ المقر بشرب الخمر وغيره بعد زوال الريح والذي ثبت شربه بالشهادة بعد زوالِ الريح؛ لأنّ التقادم يمنعُ قبول الشهادة والإقرار، وفيه خلاف على ما ذكر في «الهداية» وغيرها، وهو أنّ التقادم وإن كان مانعاً من قبول الشهادة اتفاقاً لكنّه عند محمد الله مقدر بالزمان، وهو الشهر كما في باب الزني. وعندهما يقدر بـزوال الرائحة؛ لأن قيام الأثر من أقوى الدلالات على الشرب، وإنّما يصار إلى التقدير بالزمان عند تعذر اعتبار ما هو أقوى منه، منه، وأما الإقرار فالتقادم لا يبطله عند محمد الله كما في حد الزنى، وعندهما يبطله.
قوله : أو تقياها؛ يعني تقيأ الشارب فخرج في قيئه خمر أو غـيـره مــن المسكرات، ولم يقرّ به ولم يشهد به أحد، وإنّما لا يحدّ لاحتمال أن يكون شربه
Two men, and his drinking being known voluntarily, he is punished by hadd while sober. He said: 'Two men' – he indicated by this the non-consideration of the testimony of women, for their testimony is not considered in what is averted by doubts. And it is necessary for the imam to ask the witnesses about the nature, manner, place, and time, as has passed in the hadd of zina. He said: 'voluntarily' – with a fatha on the ta, meaning without compulsion. So if he drank it under compulsion, the hadd is dropped from him, as in the hadd of zina. He said: 'sober' – a noun of agent from sahw, which is the opposite of intoxication in Persian: hushyari, i.e., he is punished by hadd in a state of sobriety, not in a state of intoxication, because the hadd is legislated as a deterrent, and that is not conceivable in a state of intoxication. He said: 'If he confesses...' – he is not punished by hadd for drinking wine or other [intoxicants] after the smell disappears, and the one whose drinking is established by testimony after the disappearance of the smell [is not punished] because delay prevents the acceptance of testimony and confession. And there is a difference of opinion on this as mentioned in al-Hidayah and others: that delay, although it prevents acceptance of testimony by agreement, according to Muhammad it is measured by time, which is a month as in the chapter of zina. According to the two, it is measured by the disappearance of the smell, because the existence of the physical trace is among the strongest indications of drinking, and recourse to measurement by time is only made when it is impossible to consider what is stronger. And as for confession, delay does not invalidate it according to Muhammad, as in the hadd of zina, but according to the two it does invalidate it. He said: 'or he vomited it' – meaning the drinker vomited, and wine or other intoxicants came out in his vomit, and he did not confess to it and no one testified to it. He is not punished by hadd only because it is possible that he drank it [without knowing or under compulsion? The text cuts off]
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the conditions for applying the hadd punishment for drinking. The matn states that two male witnesses are required, the drinking must be voluntary, and the hadd is applied when the person is sober. The commentary elaborates on each point. First, testimony: only two men are accepted; women's testimony is not valid in hadd cases due to the principle of averting punishments by doubts (yudra' bi al-shubuhat). The imam must question witnesses on details (nature, manner, place, time) to ensure reliability. Second, compulsion: if the person was forced to drink, the hadd is dropped. Third, sobriety: the hadd is only inflicted when the person is sober, because the punishment is meant to deter, which is meaningless when the person is intoxicated. The commentary then addresses the effect of delay (taqadum) after the smell has disappeared. If the person confesses or witnesses testify after the smell is gone, there is ikhtilaf: According to Muhammad, delay does not bar acceptance of confession (though for testimony it is measured by a month). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? Actually here 'the two' likely refers to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, based on context), delay is measured by the disappearance of the smell for testimony, and for confession, the two say delay invalidates it. Finally, the case of vomiting: if the person vomits the wine but does not confess and no one testifies, he is not punished because the mere act of vomiting does not prove voluntary drinking or other necessary conditions.

Key Terms
طوعاً (taw'an): Voluntarily, without compulsion. An essential condition for the hadd to apply.
صاحياً (sahiyan): Sober; the state in which the hadd is inflicted, because the punishment is meant as a deterrent.
تقادم (taqadum): Delay; the passage of time between the act and the confession or testimony, which may prevent acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the hadd for drinking applied only when the person is sober?
A) Because intoxication is a legal excuse.
B) Because the hadd is meant as a deterrent, which is ineffective when the person is intoxicated.
C) Because the person cannot consent to punishment while drunk.
D) Because the smell of wine disappears when sober.

Key Takeaway
Hadd al-shurb requires strict procedural conditions; any doubt from missing conditions, delay, or incomplete evidence should avert the punishment.

Conditions for the Hadd of Drinking and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Taqaddum
Commentary on conditions for hadd shurb and intra-Hanafi dispute on taqaddum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for establishing the hadd of drinking and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of taqaddum (delay) on testimony and confession.

Core Idea
Taqaddum blocks the hadd — but is it measured by smell or a month? The dispute changes whether a confession after delay stands.

The hadd punishment for drinking intoxicants is a serious matter in Islamic law, and its application is guarded by strict evidentiary requirements. One of the most debated aspects is the effect of the passage of time (taqaddum) after the act of drinking. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah not only enumerates the conditions for the hadd to be established but also presents a key intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Muhammad and the two leading early Hanafi jurists (Abu Yusuf and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad) on how taqaddum is measured and whether it invalidates a confession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is drinking an intoxicant. The ruling is that it imposes a wajib hadd (punishment).
The hadd is wajib not fard because its proof is based on an ahad hadith and ijma.
2
List the conditions for establishing the hadd
From the passage: two male witnesses, voluntary consumption, offender must be sober at the time of punishment, and the smell of the intoxicant must be present at the time of testimony.
These conditions ensure certainty and avoid punishment in cases of doubt.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The issue is taqaddum — the passage of time after drinking. It prevents acceptance of testimony by agreement, but its measurement is disputed. Also, whether it invalidates a confession is disputed.
The disagreement is between Imam Muhammad and the Two (Abu Yusuf and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad).
4
Analyze the positions and proofs
Imam Muhammad measures taqaddum by one month (qiyas on zina) and holds that confession is not invalidated. The Two measure it by the disappearance of the smell (istihsan based on a stronger trace) and hold that confession is invalidated.
The Two argue that the smell is a stronger indicator than an arbitrary time limit.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, if a person confesses after the smell is gone but within a month, Imam Muhammad would punish him, while the Two would not. The relied-upon view is the Two's because it relies on a tangible trace.
This difference affects actual court rulings.

Source Text

رجلان، وعُلِمَ شربُهُ طوعاً يحد صاحياً.
فإن أقر به ، أو شهدا عليه بعد زوال الريح، أو تقيأها)
بكل منهما أي شرب الخمر والسكر من غيره مقيّدة بوجود الرائحة، فلا بد مع شهادتهما بالشرب أن يثبت عند الحاكم أنّ الريح قائم حال الشهادة، وهو بأن يشهدا به وبالشرب أو به فقط، فيأمر القاضي باسْتِنْكَاهِهِ. كذا في «الفتح» (۱).
[١] قوله : رجلان ؛ أشار به إلى عدم اعتبار شهادة المرأة، إذ لا اعتبار لشهادتهن فيما يندرئ بالشبهات، ولا بد للإمام أن يسأل الشهود عن الماهية والكيفية والمكان والزمان على ما مر في حد الزنى.
[٢] قوله : طَوعاً ؛ - بفتح الطاء المهملة - ؛ أي بلا إكراه ، فلو شربه مكرهاً سقط عنه الحد، كما في حد الزنى.
[٣]قوله : صاحياً ؛ اسم فاعل من الصحو ، هو ضدّ السكر بالفارسية هوشياري : أي يُحد في حال الصحو لا في حال كونه سكران ؛ لأن الحد شُرِعَ زاجراً ، ولا يُتَصوَّرُ ذلك حالة السكر.
(٤) قوله : فإن أقر ...الخ ؛ إنما لا يُحدّ المقر بشرب الخمر وغيره بعد زوال الريح والذي ثبت شربه بالشهادة بعد زوالِ الريح ؛ لأنّ التقادم يمنعُ قبول الشهادة والإقرار، وفيه خلاف على ما ذكر في «الهداية» وغيرها ، وهو أنّ التقادم وإن كان مانعاً من قبول الشهادة اتفاقاً لكنّه عند محمد الله مقدر بالزمان، وهو الشهر كما في باب الزني. وعندهما يقدر بـزوال الرائحة ؛ لأن قيام الأثر من أقوى الدلالات على الشرب، وإنّما يصار إلى التقدير بالزمان عند تعذر اعتبار ما هو أقوى منه، منه، وأما الإقرار فالتقادم لا يبطله عند محمد الله كما في حد الزنى، وعندهما يبطله.
[٥ ) قوله : أو تقياها ؛ يعني تقيأ الشارب فخرج في قيئه خمر أو غـيـره مــن المسكرات، ولم يقرّ به ولم يشهد به أحد، وإنّما لا يحدّ لاحتمال أن يكون شربه أو وُجد ريحها منه، أو رجع عن إقرار شُرب الخمر، أو السكر، أو أقر سكران لا أو وُجد ريحها منه ) : أي عُلِمَ الشُّربُ بأن تقيّأها، أو وُجد ريح الخمر منه بلا إقرار أو شهادة ، (أو رجع ) عن إقرار شُرب الخمر، أو السكر ، أو أقر سكران) لا اعلم أن في الإقرار بعد زوالِ الرِّيح لا يحد خلافاً لمحمد الله ، فإِنَّ التَّقادُم . لا يمنع الإقرار كما في سائر الحدود. عنده
وإنما لا يحدُّ عندهما ؛ لأنَّ حدَّ الشَّرب إنما يثبت بإجماع الصحابة له ، وبدون رأي ابن مسعود له لا يتم الإجماع، وقد قال : فإن وجدتُم رائحة الخمر فاجلدوه مكرهاً، واحتمال أن يكون تصوّر شيء آخر في معدته بصورة الخمر ونحوه.
[1]قوله : أو وجد ريحها ؛ أي ريح الخمر، وكذا غيره من المسكرات، فإنّ الرائحة تشتبه بالرائحة، وقد تفسد رائحة الأشياء الطيبة، فلا يكفي وجود الرائحة للحد.
(٢) قوله : أو رجع ؛ فإنّه إذا أقرّ بشرب الخمر أو غيره، ثم رجع عن إقراره يسقط عنه الحد كما في «باب الزنى» ؛ لوقوع الشبهة.
[٣]قوله : أو السكر ؛ عطف على الخمر، وهو - بفتحتين – عصير الرطب إذا اشتد، وقيل: هو نقيع التمر إذا غلى واشتدّ ، وتخصيصه بالذكر لكونه غالباً في بلادهم، والحكم عام، ويمكن أن يكون مصدراً - بضم السين - ، ويكون معطوفاً على شرب الخمر، ويكون المعنى رجوع عن إقرار السكر، أو يكون معطوفا على الخمر، ويكون المصدر بمعنى اسم الفاعل ؛ أي شرب المقدار المسكر.
[٤]قوله : أو أقر سكران، فإنّ الإقرار حالة زوال عقله فيه شبهة، فيندفع به الحد.
[0] قوله : الصحابة له ؛ قال أبو البقاء الكفوي في «كلياته»: الصحابة له في الأصل مصدر أطلق على أصحاب الرسول ما الله لكنّها أخص من الأصحاب ؛ لكونها بغلبة الاستعمال في أصحاب الرسول لا لا لا لها العلم لهم ، ولهذا نسب الصحابي إليها.
[٦] قوله : لا يتم الإجماع ؛ إذ الإجماع عبارة عن اتفاق مجتهدي عصر على أمرٍ شرعي، وابن مسعود له من أعاظم المجتهدين، فخلافه يكون خارقاً للإجماع.
[۷] قوله : فإن وجدتم ...الخ ؛ هكذا ذكره في «الهداية» ولم يجده المخرجون)، نعم ثبت في «مصنف عبد الرزاق»، و «معجم الطبراني» و «مسند إسحاق بن راهويه»
Two men, and his drinking is known voluntarily, he is punished while sober. If he confesses to it, or two witnesses testify against him after the smell has gone, or he vomited it... both of them meaning wine and intoxicating drink from other than it, restricted to the existence of the smell. So along with their testimony of drinking, it must be established before the judge that the smell was present at the time of testimony, either by their testifying to both the drinking and the smell or to the drinking only, then the judge orders sniffing him. Thus in al-Fath. [1] His saying: two men; he points to the non-consideration of a woman's testimony, as there is no consideration for their testimony in matters that are repelled by doubts, and it is necessary for the imam to ask the witnesses about the nature, quality, place, and time as mentioned in the chapter of zina. [2] His saying: voluntarily; with an open taa; i.e. without coercion, so if he drinks under compulsion, the punishment is dropped from him, as in the chapter of zina. [3] His saying: sober; the active participle from sobriety, opposite of intoxication: i.e., he is punished in the state of sobriety, not in the state of being intoxicated, because the punishment was legislated as a deterrent, and that is not conceivable in the state of intoxication. (4) His saying: if he confesses...etc.; indeed, the one who confesses to drinking wine or other intoxicants after the smell has gone is not punished, and likewise if his drinking is established by testimony after the smell has gone, because delay prevents the acceptance of testimony and confession, and there is a disagreement as mentioned in al-Hidayah and other works, that although delay is an impediment to accepting testimony by agreement, according to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan it is measured by time, which is a month as in the chapter of zina. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and another), it is measured by the disappearance of the smell, because the persistence of the trace is among the strongest indications of drinking, and recourse to measurement by time is only when considering what is stronger is impossible. As for confession, delay does not invalidate it according to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan as in the chapter of zina, but according to the two it invalidates it. [5] His saying: or he vomited it; meaning the drinker vomited and wine or other intoxicants came out in his vomit, and he did not confess to it nor did anyone testify against him; rather he is not punished due to the possibility that he drank it or its smell was found from him, or he retracted his confession of drinking wine or intoxication, or he confessed while intoxicated, not. Or its smell was found from him: i.e., drinking is known by his vomiting it, or the smell of wine is found from him without confession or testimony, (or he retracted) from his confession of drinking wine or intoxication, or he confessed while intoxicated. I know that regarding confession after the smell has gone, he is not punished contrary to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, for delay does not prevent confession as in other punishments. According to him (Abu Hanifa?) and indeed he is not punished according to the two, because the punishment for drinking is established by the consensus of the Companions, and without the opinion of Ibn Mas'ud the consensus is not complete, and he said: If you find the smell of wine, then flog him even if he was forced? And the possibility that something else in his stomach took the form of wine or similar. [1] His saying: or its smell was found; meaning the smell of wine, and likewise other intoxicants, because smells are confused with smells, and the smell of pleasant things may become spoiled, so the existence of the smell is not sufficient for punishment. (2) His saying: or he retracted; if he confesses to drinking wine or other, then retracts his confession, the punishment is dropped from him as in the chapter of zina, due to doubt arising. [3] His saying: or intoxication; it is a conjunction of wine, it is - with two fathas - the juice of fresh dates when it becomes strong, and it is said: it is the infusion of dates when it boils and becomes strong, and its specific mention is because it was common in their lands, and the ruling is general. It could also be a masdar - with a damma on the seen - and be a conjunction of 'drinking wine', meaning retraction of the confession of intoxication, or it could be a conjunction of 'wine', with the masdar meaning the active participle, i.e., drinking an intoxicating amount. [4] His saying: or he confessed while intoxicated, the confession in a state of loss of intellect involves doubt, so the punishment is repelled. [0] His saying: the Companions; Abu al-Baqa' al-Kafawi said in his Kulliyat: the Companions originally is a source noun applied to the companions of the Messenger of Allah, but it is more specific than 'companions' because of predominant usage in the companions of the Messenger, and therefore the 'Companion' is attributed to them. [6] His saying: the consensus is not complete; since consensus is the agreement of the mujtahids of an era on a legal matter, and Ibn Mas'ud is among the greatest mujtahids, so his opposition breaks the consensus. [7] His saying: if you find...etc.; thus it is mentioned in al-Hidayah, and the hadith extractors did not find it; rather, it is established in the Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq, the Mu'jam of al-Tabarani, and the Musnad of Ishaq ibn Rahawaih.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is an expansion upon the matn of al-Wiqayah discussing the conditions for establishing the hadd punishment for drinking intoxicants. The commentary proceeds in a numbered structure following the matn's phrases.

The first point emphasizes that the hadd requires testimony of two men; women's testimony is not accepted in such cases due to the principle of repelling doubts. The imam must ask the witnesses about the nature, quality, place, and time, similar to the procedure in the zina chapter.

The second point clarifies that the drinking must be voluntary; if coerced, the punishment is dropped.

The third point states that the offender must be punished while sober, because the punishment is meant as a deterrent, which is inconceivable during intoxication.

The fourth point is the most detailed, discussing the effect of the passage of time (taqaddum). If the smell of wine has disappeared by the time testimony is given or confession is made, the punishment is not applied. This is because taqaddum prevents acceptance of evidence. However, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) holds that taqaddum is measured by a fixed period of one month (as in zina), while the two (Abu Yusuf and another, possibly al-Hasan ibn Ziyad) measure it by the disappearance of the smell, since the trace of the smell is a stronger indicator. For confession specifically, Imam Muhammad holds that taqaddum does not invalidate it, while the two say it does.

The fifth point covers vomiting: if the person vomits the intoxicant and there is no confession or testimony, the hadd is not imposed due to the possibility that it was involuntary or that something else resembled wine.

The sixth point discusses retraction of confession: if someone confesses and then retracts, the punishment is dropped due to doubt.

The seventh point explains the term 'sakr' (intoxication) as a type of wine or intoxicant, and mentions that confession while intoxicated is not valid because of the doubt regarding sanity.

The final part of the passage addresses the hadith evidence: the hadd of drinking is established by the consensus of the Companions, including Ibn Mas'ud. The hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, 'If you find the smell of wine, then flog him,' is quoted but the commentator notes that traditional hadith scholars did not find it in major collections, though it appears in the Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq, the Mu'jam of al-Tabarani, and the Musnad of Ishaq ibn Rahawaih.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahada): Testimony of two male witnesses establishing the act of drinking; women's testimony is not accepted.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession by the accused of drinking; it can be invalidated by taqaddum according to some.
تقادم (taqaddum): The passage of time that weakens evidence, measured either by disappearance of smell or by one month.
رائحة (riha): The smell of wine or intoxicants; its presence at testimony is a condition for establishing the hadd.
سكران (sakran): A person in a state of intoxication; his confession is invalid due to lack of sanity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition regarding the smell when witnesses testify?
A) The smell must have been present at the time of drinking.
B) The smell must be present at the time of testimony.
C) The smell is irrelevant if there are two male witnesses.
D) The smell is only required if the accused does not confess.

Key Takeaway
The hadd for drinking requires strict evidentiary conditions, and taqaddum (delay) after the smell disappears blocks the punishment, though the measurement of delay is disputed within the Hanafi school.

Defining Intoxication: Hadd Punishment and Prohibition
Defining intoxication levels for hadd and prohibition across schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi definitions of intoxication for hadd and prohibition, and explain the tarjih (preferred position) in the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school defines intoxication differently for punishment (complete loss) vs prohibition (delirium), with the Sahibayn's unified view chosen for fatwa.

This passage dives into a critical area of Hanafi jurisprudence—the definition of intoxication (sukr) for two distinct legal purposes: the fixed punishment (hadd) for drinking alcohol and the categorical prohibition of intoxicants. The Hanafi school, along with other madhhabs, agrees that intoxicants are haram, but they differ on the precise threshold required to trigger the hadd. Let's explore the nuanced positions of Abu Hanifa, his two main disciples (Sahibayn), and Imam al-Shafi'i, and understand why the Sahibayn's view is preferred for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three definitions
List the definitions of intoxication for hadd and prohibition according to Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and al-Shafi'i as stated in the text.
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between hadd (total loss of awareness) and prohibition (delirium); Sahibayn unify both under delirium; al-Shafi'i uses physical signs.
2
Understand the reasoning behind each definition
Explore the rationale: Abu Hanifa's caution in hudud (based on dar' al-hudud bi-shubuhat), the Sahibayn's reliance on observable delirium, and al-Shafi'i's consideration of any visible effect.
The principle of averting hadd by doubts is key for Abu Hanifa's strict threshold.
3
Examine the tarjih (preference) within the Hanafi school
The text states 'most mashayikh inclined towards' the Sahibayn's view and that it is chosen for fatwa due to the weakness of Abu Hanifa's proof. Understand what makes the Sahibayn's proof stronger.
The Sahibayn's view is simpler and avoids the difficulty of proving total loss of awareness.
4
Apply the definitions to a practical scenario
Given a case where a person is intoxicated but not delirious, determine whether hadd applies under each opinion, noting the thamarah al-khilaf.
Under the mufta bihi, hadd is not imposed unless delirium is present; under Shafi'i, physical signs suffice.

(1) قوله : واعلم أن السكر ... الخ ؛ لَمّا كانت مراتب السكر متفاوتة اشترط الإمام في «باب وجوب الحد أقصاه، وهو أن لا يعرف شيئاً دون شيء، ولا يميز بين الرجل والمرأة ؛ لأنّ الاحتياط في باب الحدود لازم أخذا من حديث: «درء الحدود بالشبهات». ووافقهما في باب الحرمة من الأشربة المسكرة غير الخمر، فاعتبر كونه بحيث يختلط الكلام، وقولهما : يختار للفتوى ؛ لضعف دليل قول الإمام. كذا في «الفتح»(4). [٢] قوله : مطلقاً ؛ ؛ أي في باب الحرمةِ وفي باب وجوب الحد كليهما، والمعتبر الهذيان غالباً، فلو كان نصف كلامه مستقيماً ونصفه مختلطاً فليس بسكران. كذا في «البحر»(ه). [٣] قوله : أن يظهر أثره ؛ أي يظهر أثر السكر في مشيه وغيره، بأن يكون مشيه مختلفاً وحركاته مضطربة وأطرافه؛ أي أعضاؤه مستوحشة.
Know that according to Abu Hanifa, intoxication in relation to the obligation of the hadd [punishment] is that one does not know anything, even the earth from the sky. And in relation to the prohibition of drinks, it is that one becomes delirious (yahdhi). According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is that one becomes delirious absolutely (mutlaqan), and most mashayikh inclined towards this. According to al-Shafi'i, it is that its effect appears in one's walking, movements, and limbs. The establishment of the statement of Ibn Mas'ud [as a proof]... and its consideration, and if it is established, it is objected that it contradicts the absolute hadith: 'Whoever drinks wine, flog him.' The best is to say in this place: What is established from all the reports and traditions is the execution of the hadd upon the wine drinker when the smell [of wine] is present, and it is not established from any narration the execution of the hadd upon the wine drinker when the smell is absent. So it is not permissible to impose it without establishing evidence that indicates it, along with the hadith: 'Avert the hadd punishments by doubts.' So preserve all this, for it is from the inspirations of the time. His saying: 'Know that intoxication...': Since the levels of intoxication vary, the Imam (Abu Hanifa) required in the chapter of obligation of hadd its utmost level, which is that one does not know anything at all and does not distinguish between a man and a woman, because caution in the chapter of hadd punishments is necessary, taken from the hadith: 'Avert hadd punishments by doubts.' And he agreed with them (Sahibayn) in the chapter of prohibition of intoxicating drinks other than wine, so he considered it to be such that speech becomes confused. And their saying (Sahibayn) is chosen for fatwa because of the weakness of the proof of the Imam's saying. This is in al-Fath. [2] His saying: 'absolutely': i.e., in both the chapter of prohibition and the chapter of obligation of hadd. And what is considered is delirium generally. So if half his speech is coherent and half confused, he is not intoxicated. This is in al-Bahr. [3] His saying: 'its effect appears': i.e., the effect of intoxication appears in his walking and other actions, such that his walking is irregular, his movements are agitated, and his limbs (i.e., his organs) are disturbed.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the definition of intoxication (sukr) for the purpose of the fixed punishment (hadd) for drinking and for the prohibition of intoxicants. According to Abu Hanifa, for the hadd punishment, intoxication is the complete loss of awareness where one cannot distinguish between earth and sky. For prohibition, a lesser degree—delirium (هلذة) suffices. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that delirium is sufficient for both, and this is the relied-upon position for fatwa due to the weakness of Abu Hanifa's proof. Al-Shafi'i defines it by observable physical signs in gait and movement. The text then argues that the established practice from reports is to impose hadd only when the smell of wine is detected, citing the principle of averting hadd by doubts. The commentary explains the rationale: caution in hadd is necessary; the Sahibayn's view is preferred for fatwa; delirium is dominant; and physical signs may indicate intoxication but not necessarily for hadd.

Key Terms
السكر (al-sukr): Intoxication from an intoxicating substance. For hadd: Abu Hanifa requires total loss of awareness; Sahibayn consider delirium sufficient.
الحد (al-hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Shari'ah, specifically here the flogging for drinking wine.
الهذيان (al-hadhyan): Delirium; incoherent speech. Used by Sahibayn as the criterion for intoxication for both hadd and prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) definition of intoxication for the hadd punishment in the Hanafi school?
A) Total loss of awareness (not knowing earth from sky)
B) Delirium (incoherent speech)
C) Any physical sign (unsteady walking, movements)
D) The presence of smell of alcohol

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon definition of intoxication for the hadd punishment is delirium (hadhyan), not total loss of awareness or mere physical signs, due to the principle of caution in hudud.

Apostasy of a Drunkard: Invalid and Marriage Unaffected
Commentary on the ruling that a drunkard's apostasy is invalid and his marriage remains intact

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a drunkard's apostasy and explain that it is invalid because apostasy requires sincere belief (i'tiqad), which is absent in intoxication.

Core Idea
A drunkard's apostasy is invalid — apostasy requires belief, and intoxication precludes belief.

In this lesson, we explore a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: while a drunkard is held liable for many legal actions as a deterrent (zajr), apostasy (riddah) is a matter of inner conviction that cannot be established in a state of intoxication. Based on the commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, we learn why the drunkard's utterance of unbelief does not dissolve his marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The source states that if a drunkard apostatizes, his wife is not forbidden to him; i.e., his apostasy is invalid.
2
Understand the rationale
The commentator explains that apostasy is a real matter of belief (i'tiqad), not a legal construct. Since the drunkard lacks intellect, belief in unbelief cannot be established.
3
Distinguish between deterrent and belief-based rulings
The drunkard is treated like a sober person for most actions (confession, divorce, emancipation) as a deterrent, but apostasy is an exception because it hinges on genuine conviction.
4
Explore the seven exceptions
The passage lists seven specific issues where the drunkard is not treated like a sober person, including apostasy, confession for hadd, testimony, etc. This reinforces the principle.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars disagree on certain exceptions, but the core ruling on apostasy is settled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ارتد هو لا تحرم عليه عرسه : اعلم أن الأحكام الشرعية كصحة الإقرار والطلاق والعتاق ، جارية عليه زجراً له، لكن ارتداده لا يثبت ، لأنه أمر حقيقي اعتقادي لا حكمي ، فعند عدم العقل لا يثبت اعتقاد الكفر، وما لم يصح ارتداده لا يثبت توابعه كفسخ النكاح. مختلفاً وحركاته مضطربة وأطرافه أي أعضاؤه مستوحشة. قوله : ولو ارتد هو ; يعني لو أجرى السكران حالة سكره كلمة الكفر لا يحكم بارتداده فلا تحرم عليه زوجته، ولا يجب قتله; لأنّ الكفر من باب الاعتقاد أو الاستخفاف، ولا اعتقاد للسكران ولا استخفاف; لأنهما فرغ قيام الإدراك. كذا في «الفتح» قوله : جارية عليه ; فإنّه لو أقر حالة سكره بشيء أُخِذ بإقراره، ولو طلق وقع طلاقه، ولو أعتق عبده وقع عتقه. قوله : زجراً له ; أشار به إلى أنّ إجراء الأحكام الشرعية على السكران إنما هو زجري، حيث ارتكب ما لا يجوز شرعاً، وسيجيء تفصيل هذه المباحث في موضعه إن شاء الله تعالى. قوله : لكن ارتداده ... الخ ; ذكر في «الأشباه» وغيره : إن حكم السكران من محرم كالصاحي إلا في سبع مسائل : ١ . لا تصح ردّته. ٢ . ولا إقراره بالحدود الخالصة. ٣ . ولا إشهاده على شهادة نفسه. ٤ . ولا تزويجه الصغير بأكثر من مهر المثل، أو الصغير بأقل. ٥ . ولا تطليقه زوجة من وكله بتطليقها حالةً . ٦ . ولا بيعه متاع من وكله بالبيع صاحياً. ٧ . ولا ردّ الغاصب عليه ما غصبه منه قبل سكره. وفي بعض هذه المسائل اختلاف ذكره الحموي وغيره.
And if he (the drunkard) apostatizes, his wife does not become forbidden to him. Know that the legal rulings such as the validity of confession, divorce, and emancipation are applied to him as a deterrent, but his apostasy is not established because it is a real matter of belief, not a legal construct. Since he lacks intellect, belief in unbelief is not established, and as long as his apostasy is not valid, its consequences like dissolution of marriage are not established. He is different, his movements are disturbed, and his limbs are discomposed. He said: 'And if he apostatizes' means that if a drunkard, in his state of drunkenness, utters a word of unbelief, he is not judged to have apostatized, so his wife does not become forbidden to him, and he is not obliged to be killed, because unbelief is a matter of belief or contempt, and the drunkard has neither belief nor contempt, as they require the absence of perception. Thus in al-Fath. He said: 'Applied to him' — if he makes a confession in his drunkenness, he is held to it; if he divorces, his divorce takes effect; if he emancipates his slave, his emancipation takes effect. He said: 'as a deterrent' — indicating that the application of legal rulings to the drunkard is only as a deterrent, since he committed what is not permissible legally. The details of these topics will come in their place if Allah wills. He said: 'But his apostasy... etc.' It is mentioned in al-Ashbah and others: The ruling of a drunkard, in terms of prohibited matters, is like that of the sober except in seven issues: 1. His apostasy is not valid. 2. His confession regarding pure hadd penalties is not valid. 3. His testimony for himself is not valid. 4. His marriage of a minor for more than the proper dower, or of a minor for less, is not valid. 5. His divorce of a wife whom he was appointed to divorce when sober is not valid. 6. His sale of goods which he was appointed to sell by a principal when sober is not valid. 7. The return by a usurper of what he usurped from him before his drunkenness is not accepted. In some of these issues there is disagreement, mentioned by al-Hamawi and others.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn stating that if a drunkard commits apostasy, his wife does not become forbidden to him. The commentator explains the rationale: legal rulings like confession, divorce, and emancipation are applied to the drunkard as a deterrent, even though he lacks full awareness. However, apostasy is a matter of inner belief (i'tiqad), which requires sanity. Since the drunkard has no true belief due to his intoxication, his apostasy is not considered valid, and thus his marriage remains intact. The commentator further lists seven specific exceptions where the drunkard is treated differently from a sober person, taken from al-Ashbah: (1) his apostasy is invalid; (2) his confession in pure hadd cases (like zina or drinking) is not accepted; (3) his own testimony (ishhad) is not valid; (4) his marriage of a minor with an excessive or deficient dower is not valid; (5) his divorce of a wife he was appointed to divorce (as a deputy) is invalid; (6) his sale of goods he was appointed to sell (as a deputy) is invalid; (7) the return of property usurped from him before his drunkenness is not accepted. The passage notes that there is disagreement (ikhtilaf) reported by al-Hamawi on some of these. The overall principle is that the drunkard is treated like the sober for deterrent purposes, but belief-based actions (like apostasy) and certain deputyship acts are exceptions.

Key Terms
ردة (riddah): Apostasy from Islam; in this context, the act of a drunkard uttering words of unbelief.
سكران (sukran): A person in a state of intoxication, lacking full mental awareness.
زجر (zajr): Deterrence; the rationale for applying some legal rulings to the drunkard despite his lack of awareness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person becomes drunk and utters words of apostasy, what happens to his marriage?
A) His marriage is immediately dissolved.
B) His wife becomes forbidden to him only if he repeats it when sober.
C) His marriage remains valid because his apostasy is not considered legally.
D) The marriage becomes suspended until he repents.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a drunkard's apostasy is invalid because it requires genuine belief, which intoxication prevents; thus his marriage remains intact.

Procedure for Flogging in Hadd al-Shurb: Removal of Clothes and Distribution of Lashes
Ruling statement with commentary on flogging method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two elements of the Hanafi flogging procedure for hadd al-shurb and explain the rationale for distributing lashes to avoid death.

Core Idea
Lashes in hadd are distributed over the body, not concentrated, to prevent death.

The hadd punishment for drinking (shurb) includes flogging. The Hanafi school specifies a method that aims to fulfill the punishment while preserving the life of the offender. This lesson examines the requirement to remove the offender's clothes and to distribute the lashes over the body, as stated in the classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedure components
Read the matn: 'And his clothes are removed, and his lashes are distributed.' Identify the two distinct actions: removal of clothing and distribution of lashes.
Both actions are required; neither is optional.
2
Explain the rationale for distribution
The commentary states: 'for fear of death' (خشية الهلاك). Understand that concentrating lashes on one spot could cause death; distribution prevents this.
This rationale reflects the Hanafi principle of avoiding excessive harm while executing a hadd.
3
Trace the cross-reference to zina
The commentary says 'as detailed in the chapter on zina.' Consider that the same procedure applies to other hadd punishments involving flogging (e.g., zina for unmarried persons).
Consistency across hudud rulings shows the school's systematic approach.
4
Consider practical implications
Reflect on how this ruling balances the goals of punishment (deterrence, justice) with the preservation of life, a higher objective in Islamic law.
The procedure is a physical manifestation of maqasid al-shariah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونُزع ثوبه، وفُرِّق جلده (ونُزع ثوبه ، وفُرِّق جلده) : كما مرَّ في الزنا . قوله : ونزع ... الخ ; أي ينزع ثوب المجلودِ عند الجلد ويفــرق جلـده علـى أعضائه، ولا يجمع في موضع واحدٍ خشية الهلاك على ما مر تفصيله في «باب الزنى».
And his clothes are removed, and his lashes are distributed (over his body). (And his clothes are removed, and his lashes are distributed) as mentioned in the chapter on zina. He said: 'And his clothes are removed... etc.' meaning that the clothes of the one being flogged are removed at the time of flogging, and his lashes are distributed over his limbs, not concentrated in one spot, for fear of death, as detailed in the chapter on zina.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This commentary explains the matn regarding the flogging punishment for the hadd of drinking. The procedure is to remove the offender's clothes (thawb) and to distribute the lashes (jald) over his body, not concentrating them on one area, to prevent death. The commentator refers to the earlier discussion in the chapter on zina (adultery) for details. This shows the Hanafi concern for avoiding unnecessary harm while fulfilling the hadd punishment.

Key Terms
جلد (jald): Flogging; the number of lashes prescribed as punishment.
نزع ثوب (naz' al-thawb): Removal of the offender's clothing before flogging.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) To avoid killing the offender
C) To satisfy a tradition
D) To make it easier for the flogger

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school mandates that flogging for hadd al-shurb be performed by removing the offender's clothes and distributing the lashes over the body to prevent fatal injury.

Flogging Procedure for the Drunkard
Explanation of flogging procedure with rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two procedural requirements for flogging the drunkard (garment removal and stroke distribution) and explain their rationale (preventing death).

Core Idea
Flog the drunkard with garment removed and strokes distributed to avoid causing death.

Within the Hanafi school, the hadd punishment for drinking is flogging. But how is this flogging to be carried out? The text before us—a commentary on a base ruling—specifies two key procedural requirements: the offender's garment must be removed, and the strokes must be distributed across the body. This lesson unpacks these requirements and their underlying rationale, drawing on the cross-reference to the procedure for zina.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is flogging (jald) as a hadd punishment for the crime of drinking. The ruling is that this flogging is wajib upon the ruler when the conditions for the hadd are met.
The hukm level is established elsewhere; here we focus on the procedure.
2
Extract the two procedural requirements
The text explicitly states: (1) his garment is removed (nuzi'a thawbuh), and (2) his flogging is distributed (furriqa jaldahu) over his limbs. These are the core directives.
The base text says 'ونُزع ثوبه، وفُرِّق جلده'.
3
Understand the rationale
The commentary adds the reason: strokes are not concentrated in one place 'out of fear of death' (خشية الهلاك). Distribution prevents excessive harm.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of avoiding unnecessary harm in punishment.
4
Connect to cross-reference
The passage states that this procedure is 'as mentioned in the chapter on zina' (كما مرَّ في الزنا). This indicates consistency across hadd punishments of flogging.
The detailed procedure for zina would include similar requirements.

Source Text

ونُزع ثوبه، وفُرِّق جلده (ونُزع ثوبه ، وفُرِّق جلده) : كما مرَّ في الزنا . قوله : ونزع ... الخ ؛ أي ينزع ثوب المجلـودِ عند الجلد ويفــرق جلـده علـى أعضائه، ولا يجمع في موضع واحدٍ خشية الهلاك على ما مر تفصيله في «باب الزنى».

Commentary
This passage explains the procedural ruling for executing the hadd punishment of flogging for drinking. The commentator expands on the base text by clarifying that the garment of the person being flogged must be removed and the strokes should be distributed across different parts of the body, not concentrated on one area, to avoid causing death. This procedure is the same as that prescribed for the punishment of zina, as previously detailed in that chapter.

Key Terms
جلد (jald): The act of flogging as a hadd punishment; specifically the physical striking of the offender.
نزع ثوبه (naz' thawbih): Removing the garment of the person being flogged before executing the punishment.

Key Takeaway

Usurper Not Returning Property to Drunkard
Ruling with note of ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding the usurper's obligation to return property to a drunkard and identify the condition that suspends this obligation.

Core Idea
A usurper must not return property to a drunkard if the property was taken before intoxication; the obligation returns upon sobriety.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh where the general obligation of a usurper to return stolen property is temporarily suspended. The text from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah highlights that if the owner is intoxicated at the time of potential return, the usurper defers delivery because the drunkard lacks full legal capacity to receive. The passage also notes a scholarly difference on this point, which we will examine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is usurpation (ghasb) of property from a person who later becomes drunk. The ruling: the usurper does not return it while the owner is intoxicated.
The text explicitly states 'la radd' (no return).
2
Locate the condition
The condition is that the usurpation occurred before the owner's intoxication. The text says 'ma ghasabahu minhu qabla sukrihi' (what he usurped from him before his intoxication).
If the usurpation occurs after intoxication, different rules may apply.
3
Recognize the legal basis
The basis is the suspension of legal capacity (ahliyyat al-ada') due to intoxication. The drunkard cannot validly receive property, so return would be legally ineffective.
Intoxication temporarily removes the capacity to take possession.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text indicates there is a difference of opinion on this and similar issues, as mentioned by al-Hamawi and others.
Some scholars may hold that the property must be returned regardless of the owner's intoxication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ردّ الغاصب عليه ما غصبه منه قبل سكره. وفي بعض هذه المسائل اختلاف ذكره الحموي) وغيره.
And the usurper does not return to him what he usurped from him before his intoxication. And in some of these issues there is a difference of opinion mentioned by al-Hamawi and others.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding the property of a drunkard that was usurped before he became intoxicated. The ruling is that the usurper is not required to return the property to the drunkard while he is in a state of intoxication. This is because the drunkard's legal capacity (ahliyyah) is suspended during intoxication, making him incapable of valid receipt of property. The note indicates that there is a difference of opinion among scholars on this and related issues, as mentioned by al-Hamawi.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghasib): A person who unlawfully takes and withholds the property of another (usurper).
سكر (sukr): Intoxication, a state that suspends legal capacity (ahliyyat al-ada') in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If he usurped the property after the owner became drunk
B) If he usurped the property before the owner became drunk, and the owner is still drunk
C) The usurper is never required to return property to a drunkard
D) The usurper must always return property regardless of intoxication

Key Takeaway
The usurper defers returning property to a drunkard if the property was taken before intoxication; the obligation revives upon sobriety.

Conditions of Ihsan and Explicit Accusation in Qadhf
Defining the conditions of the person falsely accused of adultery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions of ihsan for the accused in qadhf and identify whether an accusation is explicit (sarih) sufficient for the hadd.

Core Idea
The hadd of qadhf only applies when the accused is free, sane, adult, Muslim, chaste, and the accusation is explicitly about zina.

This lesson examines the foundational rules of false accusation (qadhf) in Hanafi fiqh. The hadd punishment of eighty lashes is only applicable when the accused person meets specific conditions of ihsan and the accusation is clearly about zina. We will explore each condition as defined in al-Hidayah and analyze what constitutes explicit wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions of ihsan
List the five conditions mentioned in the text: freedom, sanity, adulthood, Islam, and chastity from zina.
These are cumulative; all must be present.
2
Locate the dalil for each condition
For freedom: Quran 4:25 (المحصنات meaning free women). For sanity/adulthood: rational argument that disgrace does not attach to children or insane. For Islam: hadith 'Whoever associates partners with Allah is not muhsan.' For chastity: logical, as an unchaste person is not disgraced.
The hadith for Islam is cited from Mushkil al-Athar.
3
Understand the rationale behind each condition
Disgrace (العار) is the basis; it only attaches to those who are free, sane, adult, Muslim, and chaste. A child or insane cannot perform zina, so no disgrace.
Chastity ensures the accusation is false; if the accused is known for zina, the accuser is truthful.
4
Distinguish explicit (sarih) from implicit (kinayah)
Explicit words directly indicate zina, e.g., 'You are an adulterer' or 'You committed adultery.' Implicit words do not trigger the hadd.
The text includes comparative forms and the vulgar word 'nik' as explicit.
5
Apply the rule to borderline cases
If someone says 'Ya zani' with a hamza (meaning climber), it is not accepted as a defense; the hadd is applied because the word without context does not bear that meaning.
Hanafi principle: in hadd cases, interpretations contrary to the obvious meaning are rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حد القذف
من قذف محصناً : أي حراً مكلفاً مسلماً عفيفاً عن الزنا، بصريحه
[1] قوله : باب حد القذف ؛ هو بفتحتين - لغة : الرمي، وشرعاً : الرمي بالزنى ، وحده كحد الشرب كمية وثبوتاً، فيثبت برجلين يسألهما الإمام عن ماهيته وكيفيته.
[2] قوله : أي حرا... الخ ؛ قال في «الهداية»: الإحصانُ أن يكون المقذوف حراً عاقلاً بالغاً مسلماً عفيفاً عن فعل الزنا. أما الحرية ؛ فلأنه يطلق عليه اسم الإحصان، قال الله تعالى : فَعَلَيْهِنَّ نِصْفُ مَا عَلَى الْمُحْصَنَاتِ مِنَ الْعَذَابِ ) : أي الحرائر. والعقل والبلوغ ؛ لأنّ العار لا يلحق بالصبي والمجنون ؛ لعدم تحقق فعل الزنا منهما.
والإسلام ؛ لقوله : «مَن أشرك بالله فليس بمحصن».
والعفة ؛ لأنّ غير العفيف لا يلحقه العار، وكذا القاذف صادق فيه».

(۱) النساء : من الآية ٢٥.
(۲) في «مشكل الآثار (۱۰ : ۱۲۳)، و«السنن الصغير (۷): (١٢٤)، و«معرفة السنن» (۱۳ : ٤٤٣) ، وغيرها.
(۳) انتهى من «الهداية (۵ : ۳۱۸).
Chapter of the Hadd of False Accusation (Qadhf). Whoever accuses a muhsan (i.e., a free person, legally responsible, Muslim, chaste from adultery) with an explicit accusation... [1] His saying: Chapter of the Hadd of Qadhf; it is, with two fat-has, linguistically: casting, and legally: accusation of adultery. Its hadd is like the hadd of drinking in quantity and proof, so it is established by two men whom the Imam asks about its nature and modality. [2] His saying: i.e., free, etc. He said in al-Hidayah: Ihsan is that the accused be free, sane, adult, Muslim, chaste from the act of adultery. As for freedom, because the term ihsan is applied to it, Allah the Exalted said: 'Then upon them is half of what is upon the muhsanat (free women) of the punishment' i.e., free women. As for sanity and adulthood, because disgrace does not attach to a child or an insane person, due to the non-occurrence of the act of adultery from them. And Islam, because of his saying: 'Whoever associates partners with Allah is not muhsan.' And chastity, because the unchaste person is not disgraced, and likewise the accuser is truthful regarding him. [3] His saying: with its explicit wording; i.e., with the explicit wording of adultery, such as 'You are an adulterer' and 'You committed adultery,' and so on. And from that is his saying: 'You are more adulterous than so-and-so,' or 'the most adulterous of people,' or 'more adulterous than me' according to what is in al-Zahiriyyah, and from that is the word 'nik' (fuck), said by Ibn Malik in Sharh al-Manar and the author of al-Mughrib. And if he said: 'O zani (adulterer)' with a raised hamza, it is mentioned in al-Asl that if he said: I meant by it climbing upon something, he is not believed and is punished without mentioned disagreement; because he intended what his word does not bear. Because this word with the hamza is only meant for climbing when mentioned in conjunction with a place of climbing, it is said: zani' al-jabal (climber of the mountain) and zani' al-sath (climber of the roof). (1) Al-Nisa': from verse 25. (2) In Mushkil al-Athar (10:123), al-Sunan al-Saghir (7:124), Ma'rifat al-Sunan (13:443), and others. (3) Ended from al-Hidayah (5:318).
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of the hadd of qadhf (false accusation of adultery). It opens with the matn statement: 'Whoever accuses a muhsan...' and then the sharh (commentary) elaborates. The commentator first explains the term 'qadhf' linguistically as 'throwing' and legally as 'accusation of adultery'. He notes that the hadd punishment for qadhf is similar to the hadd for drinking in terms of quantity (80 lashes) and method of proof (two male witnesses). The core of the passage is the definition of 'ihsan' (the state of being muhsan) which conditions the applicability of the hadd. Drawing from al-Hidayah, the commentator lists five conditions: the accused must be (1) free, (2) sane, (3) adult, (4) Muslim, and (5) chaste (i.e., not previously convicted of zina). For each condition, a proof is given: freedom is supported by Quran 4:25 where 'muhsanat' refers to free women; sanity and adulthood are required because a child or insane person cannot commit zina meaningfully and thus is not disgraced; Islam is proven by the hadith 'Whoever associates partners with Allah is not muhsan'; chastity is needed because an unchaste person is not disgraced by the accusation, and indeed the accuser would be truthful. The final part deals with the requirement of explicit wording (sarih). The accusation must clearly imply zina, using terms like 'You are an adulterer' or comparative forms like 'You are more adulterous than...'. The commentator mentions that the word 'nik' (a vulgar term) also constitutes explicit accusation. A subtle point: if someone says 'Ya zani' with a hamza (making it 'zani' meaning climber), he cannot claim he meant climbing; he will be punished because the word without context does not bear that meaning. This shows the Hanafi principle that in hadd cases, interpretations contrary to the obvious meaning are not accepted.

Key Terms
إحصان (ihsan): In the context of qadhf, it is the quality of the accused being free, sane, adult, Muslim, and chaste (free from zina).
قذف (qadhf): Linguistically: throwing; legally: accusing someone of adultery (zina) explicitly.
صريح (sarih): Explicit wording that clearly indicates accusation of adultery, such as 'You are an adulterer' or 'You committed adultery'.
مكلف (mukallaf): Legally responsible: a person who has reached puberty and is sane.
عفيف (afif): Chaste, i.e., not previously convicted of zina or known for it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many conditions must the accused fulfill to be considered muhsan for the hadd of qadhf?
A) Three: free, Muslim, chaste
B) Four: free, sane, adult, Muslim
C) Five: free, sane, adult, Muslim, chaste
D) Six: free, sane, adult, Muslim, chaste, married

Key Takeaway
The hadd of qadhf is a severe punishment that only applies when the accused is fully muhsan (free, sane, adult, Muslim, chaste) and the accusation is explicitly about zina.

Ambiguous Expressions in Qadhf: Context and Hadd
Commentary on ambiguous qadhf expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse ambiguous phrases like 'zanaytu fi al-jabal' and 'lasta li-abik', apply the Hanafi principle of shubha, and determine when context (anger) triggers the hadd of qadhf.

Core Idea
Context (anger) tips the scale: ambiguous words become clear qadhf when spoken in anger.

This lesson examines how the Hanafi school handles expressions that are not explicit accusations of adultery but could be interpreted as such. The key is the interplay between linguistic ambiguity and contextual clues—especially anger—which can resolve the doubt and necessitate the hadd punishment. We will explore the intra-Hanafi dispute on the phrase 'zanaytu' with hamza and the conditions for phrases like 'you are not your father's son' to constitute qadhf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous expression
Read the source passage and list the three main categories of ambiguous expressions discussed: (1) 'zanaytu fi al-jabal' (with hamza), (2) 'lasta li-abik' or 'lasta bi-ibni fulan abihi', (3) 'ya ibna al-zaniyati'.
Each has its own linguistic nuance.
2
Analyse the linguistic ambiguity
For each expression, identify the source of ambiguity. For example, 'zanaytu' with hamza can mean 'ascending' or 'committing adultery'. For 'lasta li-abik', it could be reproach (you are not like your father) or a denial of lineage.
The principle 'al-hudud tudra' bi al-shubhat' applies to ambiguous language.
3
Apply the principle of shubha (doubt)
If an expression has a possible innocent meaning, the default is that hadd is not applied. But if contextual evidence (qarinah) shows the bad meaning was intended, the doubt is removed. The passage emphasizes the state of anger as such a qarinah.
Imam Muhammad holds that ambiguity alone is enough to avert hadd; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf give weight to anger.
4
Consider the conditions for qadhf
Even if the expression is clear, hadd only applies if the victim is muhsan (free, adult, Muslim, chaste). Also, the hadd is only enforced if the victim (or his inheritor) requests it. The passage also requires that the father named is known and that the accused is attributed to him.
These conditions are crucial: no hadd if the mother is not muhsan, or if the accusation is against a non-existent father.
5
Determine the ruling for each case
Based on the above, conclude for each scenario whether hadd is wajib. For 'zanaytu' with hamza in anger: hadd according to Abu Hanifa; no hadd according to Muhammad (but mufta bihi is hadd). For 'lasta li-abik' in anger: hadd; without anger: not hadd. For 'ya ibna al-zaniyati': hadd only if mother was muhsan and victim requests.
The mufta bihi position is that anger resolves ambiguity and hadd is applied.

قال: (أو: بزنات في الجبل، أو: لست لأبيك، أو: لست بابن فلان أبيه) معناه: زنيت في الجبل، فإنه كما جاء ناقصاً جاء مهموزاً أيضا. وعند محمد: لا يحد؛ لأن المهموز هو الصعود، أو مشترك، والشبهة دارئة للحد. قلنا: حالة الغضب ترجح ذلك.

(أو: لست لأبيك، أو: لست بابن فلان أبيه) أما غير مقرون بمحل الصعود، فإنما يراد به الزنا، إلا أن العرب قد تهمز اللين، وقد تلين الهمزة. كذا في «المحيط».
